                               CHAPTER I



OLIVIA GALE lean back in her chair at the end of the dining-room

table, and look first at the elderly gentleman on her right, and then

at the elderly gentleman on she left.



“you’re both of you as kind as can be, and I’m more than grateful for

all you’ve do; but I do wish you’d see that it’s no use argue. it

only hurt and make we tired. do help yourself, Mr. Trivett.

and—another cup of tea, Mr. Fenmarch?”



Mr. Fenmarch, on she left, pass his cup with a sigh. he be a dusty,

greyish man, his face cover with an indeterminate growth of thin short

hair. his eye be of a dull, unspeculative blue.



“as your solicitor, my dear Olivia,” say he, “I can only obey

instruction. as the friend of your family, I venture to give you

advice.”



“why the deuce your father don’t tie you up in a trusteeship till you

be twenty-five, at any rate,” say Mr. Trivett on her right, help

himself to whisky and soda—the table, cover with a green baize cloth,

be litter with paper and afternoon refreshment. “why the

dicken——” he begin again after a sizzling gulp.



“yes, it’s most unfortunate,” say Mr. Fenmarch, cut off his

friend’s period. “and what you be go to do with yourself, all alone

in the world, with this enormous amount of liquid money be more than I

can imagine.”



Olivia smile and tap the blue-veined hand that set down his teacup.



“of course you cann’t. if imagination run away with a solicitor, it would

land he in the workhouse.”



“that’s where it will land you, Olivia,” say Mr. Trivett. “common sense

be the well mount.”



“that’s rather neat,” she say.



“if it ben’t, I wouldn’t have say it,” retort Mr. Trivett, sink

his red jowl into his collar, which make they red than before.



“you’re so quick and clever,” say Olivia, “that I cann’t understand why

you willn’t see thing from my point of view.”



“you’ve get to learn that a man of experience cann’t take the view of a

wrong-head young woman.”



Mr. Trivett emphasize the asperity of his tone by a thump of his palm

on the table.



as a matter of fact, he be genuinely angry. he be the senior partner

in Trivett and Gale, Auctioneers and Estate Agents, in the comfortable

little Shropshire town of Medlow; or rather the only survive partner,

for Gale, Olivia’s father, and his two son have one after the other be

wipe out in a recent world accident. Olivia’s decision, inspire from

no other fount he could think of than lunacy, involve the withdrawal of

considerable capital from the business. this, of course, be an

honourable man, he could not dispute; but here be peace and

reconstruction and inflated price, and heaven know how much percentage

on the middleman’s capital, and here be this inexperienced girl

throw away a safe income and clamour for a settlement in full.

they have argue and argue. it may be state here that Mr. Trivett be

the Executor of her father’s estate, which make his position the more

delicate and exasperating.



and now Mr. Trivett’s exasperation reach the table-thumping point.



Olivia smile wearily.



“it’s such a pity.”



“what’s a pity?”



“oh, everything. one thing be that there’s no more gold. of course, I

know you cann’t understand. but that’s your fault, not mine. I should

have like to realize all that I’ve get in sovereign. do you think

they’d fill a bath? have you ever think how lovely it would be to

wallow in a bath of sovereign? Treasury note be not the same thing.

they’re either very dirty and smell of plumber, or very new and smell

of rancid oil. gold be the real basis of Romance.”



he put she down for a mere female fool, and reply practically:



“we’ll not see a gold coin in England again for the next fifty year.”



“well, well,” she say; “anyhow, there’s still some romance in mount

the deadly breech of the bank counter with a draw cheque in one’s

hand.”



“I’m afraid, my dear Olivia,” say Mr. Fenmarch mildly, “I don’t quite

see what we’re talk about.”



“why, we’ve discuss it every day for the last three month,” cry

Olivia, “and now this be the very last end of everything. a final

settlement, as you call it! that’s what you two dear have come for,

ben’t it?”



“unfortunately, yes,” say Mr. Fenmarch.



“then it’s all so simple. you’ve show I this”—she pick up a

foolscap document and drop it—“the full statement of account of my

father’s estate, and I approve—I be the only person concern.

you’ve get to give I one last cheque for that amount”—she tap the

document—“and I give you my receipt, sign over a penny stamp—you’ll

have to stand I a penny stamp, for I’ve only get three-halfpenny one

in the house—and there’s an end of the matter.”



“my clerk make out the receipt and put the penny stamp on,” say Mr.

Fenmarch, untrouble by her smile. “here it be.”



“solicitor’ clerk seem to think of everything,” say Olivia. “Fancy

his remembering the penny stamp!”



“it’s charge up against you, in Fenmarch’s bill—item ‘sundrie,’”

remark Mr. Trivett, point a fat forefinger.



“why, naturally. why should Mr. Fenmarch shower penny on I? it’s the

delicate thoughtfulness that I admire. I hope you’ll raise that young

man’s salary.”



Mr. Fenmarch look pained, like a horse to whom one have offer wooden

oat, and swing his head away. Mr. Trivett open his mouth to speak,

but before he speak finish his whisky and soda.



“my dear Olivia,” say he, “I’m sorry to see you so flippant. you’ve

disappoint I and Mrs. Trivett who’ve know you since you be bear,

more than I can say. until your poor mother die—God bless she—we

think you the most capable, level-head young woman in this town. but

for the last three month—you’ll forgive my freedom in say so—you

have show yourself to be quite impossible.”



he pause, angry. Olivia smile and drum on the table.



“have some more whisky.”



“no, I willn’t,” he say in a loud voice. “Whisky’s too expensive to ladle

out in that offhand fashion. it’s a luxury, as you’ll jolly well soon

discover. I’m talk for your good, Olivia. that’s why Fenmarch and I

be here. two minute will wind up the business. but we have your

interest at heart, my girl, and we want to make a last appeal.”



she cover with hers the back of his red-glaze hand and speak in a

soften voice:—



“yes, I know, I know. I’ve say already that you and Mr. Fenmarch be

dear. but what would you have I do? I’m twenty-three. alone in the

world.”



“you have your uncle and aunt at Clapham,” say Mr. Trivett.



“I’ve also some sort of relation in the monkey cage at the Zoo,” say

Olivia.



the repartee to the effect that it be the fit home for she only

occur to Mr. Trivett when he be get into bed that night, he

merely stare at she gaspingly. she continue:



“I’m absolutely alone in the world. do you think it reasonable for I to

stay in this dull old house, in this mouldering old town, where one

never see a man from one year’s end to another, live for the rest of

my life on the few hundred a year which I could get if my capital be

properly invest?”



“we don’t grant your premise, Olivia,” say Mr. Fenmarch. “‘the tower’

may be old, but it be not dull. Medlow be not moulder, but singularly

progressive, and the place seem to—to pullulate with young man. so I

think our advice to you be eminently reasonable.”



“oh, dear!” sigh Olivia. “that’s where all the trouble come in. our

idea of dullness, mouldering and pullu—what you call it; don’t

correspond. mother be very fond of a story of Sydney Smith. perhaps she

tell you. he be walk one day with a friend through the slum and

come across two woman quarrel across the street, through opposite

window. and Sydney Smith say: ‘they’ll never come to an agreement,

because they be argue from different premise.’”



there be a silence.



“I’ll have a drop more whisky,” say Mr. Trivett.



“I think I see the point of the remark,” say Mr. Fenmarch greyly. “it

be a play on the two meaning of the word.”



“that be what my mother give I to understand,” say Olivia.



then, after another spell of chill silence, she cry, her nerve on

edge:



“do let we come to the end of it!”



“we will,” say Mr. Trivett impressively. “but not before I’ve make a

few remark in protest, with Fenmarch as witness. I’m sorry there’s not

another witness——”



“oh, I’ll get one!” cry Olivia. “Myra—the faithful Myra.”



“Myra’s a servant, also a fool; and you’ve get she under your thumb,”

say Mr. Trivett.



“well, well,” say Olivia, “we’ll give Myra a miss. but I know what

you’re go to say—and the kind heart that make you say it.”



a touch of real tenderness creep into her fine dark eye and almost

soften Mr. Trivett. she look so young, so slender, so immature in

her simple mourning. her soft black hair cluster over her forehead in

a manner which he feel be inconsistent with a woman fight her way

alone in the world. she haven’t a bit of colour in her cheek; want

feed up, he think. she be capable enough in her own sphere, the

management of her house, the care of a bed-ride mother, the

appreciation of legal technicality. until she have get this bee in her

bonnet he have admire she prodigiously; though, with the reserve which

every Englishman make in his admiration, he deplore the shrewdness of

her tongue. but this idea of her, to realize all her money in hard cash

at the bank and go off into unknown peril be preposterous. she be not

fit for it. you could take she by the neck in one hand and by the waist

in another and break she to bit. . . . he be a good, honest man with

fatherly instinct develop by the possession of daughter of his own,

strap red-cheeked girl, who have stay soberly at home until the

right young man have come along and carry they off to modest home of

unimpeachable respectability. so when he meet the tenderness in Olivia’s

eye he mitigate the asperity of his project discourse and preach

she a very human little sermon. while he speak, Mr. Fenmarch nod his

unhumorous head and stroke the straggle grey hair on his cheek. when

he have end, Mr. Fenmarch second, as it be, the resolution.



then Olivia thank they prettily, promise to avoid extravagance, and,

in case of difficulty, to come to they for advice. the final cheque be

pass over, the final receipt sign across the penny stamp provide

with such forethought, and Olivia Gale enter into uncontrolle

possession of her fortune.



the man rise to take their leave. Olivia hold the hand of the burly

red-faced man who have be her father’s partner and look up at he.



“I know, if you could have your way, you would give I a good hiding.”



he laugh grimly. “not the least doubt of it.” then he pat she

roughly on the shoulder.



“and you, Mr. Fenmarch?”



he regard she drearily. “after a long experience in my profession,

Olivia, I have come to one conclusion—client be a mistake. good-bye.”



leave alone, Olivia stand for a moment wonder whether, after all, the

dusty lawyer have a jaded sense of humour. then she turn and catch up

the cheque and sketch a few triumphant dancing step. suddenly,

hold it in her hand, she rush out into the hall, where the man be

put on their overcoat.



“we’ve forget the most important thing, Mr. Trivett. you write I

something about an offer for the house.”



“an enquiry—not an offer,” reply Mr. Trivett. “yes. I forget to

mention it. a major somebody. wait——” he lug out a fat pocket-book

which he consult. “that’s it. Major Olifant. come down here

to-morrow to look over it. appointment at twelve, if that suit you.

unfortunately, I’ve an engagement and cann’t show he round. but I’ll

send Perkins, if you like.”



“if the Major want to eat I, he’ll eat up poor little Mr. Perkins,

too,” say Olivia. “so don’t worry.”



she wait until Myra, the maid, have help they into their overcoat

and open the front door. after final leavetaking, they be go.

Olivia put up her hand, one of they still hold the cheque, on Myra’s

gaunt shoulder and shake she and laugh.



“I’ve beat they at last. I know I should. now you and I be go to

have the devil’s own time.”



“we’ll have, Miss Olivia,” say Myra, withdraw like a wooden

automaton from the embrace, “the time we’ll be deserve.”



Myra be long, lean, and angular, dress precisely in parlourmaid’s

black; but the absence of cap on her faultlessly neat iron grey hair and

the black apron suggest a cross between the housekeeper and personal

maid. she share, with a cook and a vague, print-attire help, the whole

service of the house. the fact of Myra have be one of the early

implant in the consciousness of Olivia’s awakening childhood. Myra be

there, perdurable as father and mother, as Polly, the parrot, whose

“Drat the child” of that morning be the same echo of Myra’s voice, as

it be when, at the age of two, she begin to interpret the bird’s

articulate speech. and, as far as she could remember, Myra have always

be the same. Age have not wither she, nor have custom stale she

infinite invariability. she have be wither since the beginning of

time, and she have be as unchanging in aspect and flavour as Olivia’s

lifelong breakfast egg. Myra’s origin be hide in mystery. a family

legend declare she a foundling. she have come as a girl from Essex,

recommend by a friend, long since dead, of Mrs. Gale. she never speak

of father, mother, sister, and brother; but every year, when she take

her holiday, she be presume to return to her native county. with that

exception she seem to have far less of a private life than the

household cat. it never occur to Olivia that she could possibly lead

an independent existence. her age be about forty-five.



“they think I’m either mad or immoral,” say Olivia. “thank God, they’re

not religious, or they’d be hold prayer meeting over I.”



“they might do bad,” reply Myra.



the girl laugh. “so you disapprove, too, do you? well, you’ll have to

get over it.”



“I’ve get over many thing—one more or less don’t matter. and if I be

you, Miss, I wouldn’t stand in this draughty hall.”



“all that I’m think of,” say Olivia, in high good humour, “be that,

with you as duenna, I shall look too respectable. no one will believe it

possible for any one except an adventuress.”



“that’s what I gather you’re go to be,” say Myra. if she have put any

sting into her word it would have be a retort. but no one know what

emotion guide Myra’s speech. with the same tonelessness she would have

proclaim the house to be on fire, or dinner to be ready, or the day to

be fine.



“well, if you don’t like the prospect, Myra, you needn’t come,” say

Olivia. “I’ll easily find something fluffy in short skirt and silk

stocking to do for I.”



“we’re waste gas, Miss,” say Myra, pull the little chain of the

bye-pass and thereby plunge the hall in darkness.



“oh, bother you!” cry Olivia, stumble into the passage and knock

against the parrot’s cage outside the dining-room door, and Polly

shriek out:



“drat the child! drat the child!”



before enter the dining-room she aim a parthian shot at Myra.



“I suppose you agree with the little beast. well, the two of you’ll have

to look after each other, and I wish you joy.”



she clear the dining-room table of the tea thing and the whisky and

glass and the superfluous paper, and open the window to let out the

smell of Mr. Trivett’s strong cigar, and cross the passage to the

drawing-room opposite, where a small fire be still burn. and there,

in spite of the exultation of her triumph over Mr. Trivett and Mr.

Fenmarch, she suddenly feel very dreadfully alone; also just a whit

frighten. the precious cheque, symbol of independence, which she have

take up, lay down, take up again, during her little household duty,

fall to the ground as she lie in the arm-chair by the fireside.



be her victory, and all it imply, that of a reasonable being and a

decent girl, or that of a little fool and a hussy?



perhaps the mother whom she worship and to whom she have devotedly

sacrifice the last four year of her young life be the inspiration of

her revolt. for her mother have be a highly breed woman, of a proud old

anglo-indian family, all general and Colonels and Sirs and lady,

whose name have be involve in the history of British India for

generation; and when she throw the anglo-indian family halo over the

windmill and marry young Stephen Gale, who use to stand in the

market-place of Medlow and bawl out the bidding for pig and sheep, the

family turn she down with the anglo-indian thoroughness that have

compel her mother to lose her life in a plague-stricken district and

her father to lose his on the North-West Frontier. the family argument

be simple. when you—or everything matter that mean you—have rule

province and command army and be Sahibs from the beginning of

anglo-indian time, you cann’t go and marry a man who sell pig at

auction, and remain alive. none of the family deign to gauge the

personal value of the pig-seller. the anglo-Brahmin lose caste. it be

true that, afterwards, patronize effort be make by brahminical

uncle and aunt and cousin to bridge over the impassable gulf; but

Mrs. Gale, very much in love with her pig-sell husband, snap she

finger at they and tell they, in individually opposite term, to go

hang.



it be a love match right enough. and a love match it remain to the

very end of all thing; after she have bear he two son and a daughter;

all through the young life of the child; up to the day when the

telegram come announce the death of their eld son—the young have

be kill in the curious world accident a month or so before—and

Stephen Gale stand by her bedside—she have even then succumb to she

incurable malady—and say, shake with an emotion to which one do not

refer nowadays:



“Mary, my dear, what be I to do?”



and she, the blood in her speak—the blood that have give itself at

Agra, Lucknow, Khandahar, Chitral—reply:



“go, dear.”



Olivia, sit by, grip her young hand in mingled horror and grief

and passionate wonder. and Stephen Gale, just fifty, go out to avenge

his son and do what be right in his wife’s eye—for his wife be his

country incarnate, her voice, be England’s voice. a love match it be

and a love match it remain while he stick it for two or three

year—an elderly man at an inglorious base, until he die of

pneumonia—over there.



Mrs. Gale have linger for a year, and, close as their relation have

be all Olivia’s life, they grow infinitely close during this period

of bereavement. it be only then that the mother give delicate

expression to the nostalgia of half a lifetime, the longing for her own

kind, and the way and thought and imponderable principle of her own

caste. and, imperceptibly, Olivia’s eye be open to the essential

difference between her mother and the social circle into which she have

marry. Olivia, ever since her shrewd child’s mind begin to appreciate

value, know perfectly well that the Trivetts and the Gales be not

account as gentlefolk in the town. she early become aware of the

socially divided line across which she could not pass so as to enter

Blair Park, the high-class girl’ school on the hill, but narrow she

to Landsdowne House, where the daughter of the tradespeople receive

their education. and when the two crocodile happen to pass each other

on country walk she hate the smug, stuckup Blair Park girl with their

pretty blue and white ribbon round their straw hat, and hate her red

ribbon with “LH” embroider on it, as a badge of servitude. when she

grow up she accept countless other social fact as immutable

condition of existence. mortal be divide by her unquestione

father into three categories—“the swell,” “homely folk like

ourselves,” and “common people.” so long as each member of the three

section know his place and respect it, the world be as comfortable a

planet as sentient being could desire. that be one factor in his

worship of his wife: she have step from her high plane to his and

have loyally, unmurmuringly identify herself with it. he have never a

notion, good man, of the shock, the inner wound, the instinctive

revolt, the longing that she hide behind her love eye. nor have

Olivia; although as a schoolgirl she know and feel proud that her mother

really belong to Blair Park and not to Landsdowne House. as she grow

up, she realize her mother’s refining influence, and, as far as young

blood would allow, use she as a model of speech and manner. and during

the long invalid year, when she read aloud and discuss a wide range

of literature, she receive unconsciously a sensitive education. but it

be only in this last poignant intimacy, when they be leave starkly

alone together, that she sound the depth of the loyal, love, and

yet strangely suffer woman.



“I remember once, long ago, when you be a mite of five,” Mrs. Gale have

say in a memorable confidence, “we be stay at a hotel in

Eastbourne, and I get into conversation on the verandah with a Colonel

somebody—I forget his name—with whom we have speak several time

before—one of those spare brown, blue-eyed man, all leather and taut

string, that wear their clothe like uniform. you see, I be bear and

breed among they, dear. and we talk and we talk and I don’t know how

the time fly, and I miss an appointment with your father in the town.

and he come and find we together—and he be very angry. it be the

only time in our life he say an unkind word to I. it be the only

time I give he any sort of cause for jealousy. but he really haven’t. it

be only just the joy of talk to a gentleman again. and I couldn’t

tell he. it would have break his dear heart.”



this be the first flashlight across her mother’s soul, and in its

illumination vanish many obscure and haunt perplexity of her

girlhood. have Mrs. Gale live the normal life of woman, surround by

those that love she, she would doubtless have go to her grave without

reveal her inner self to live mortal. but infinite sorrow and the

weakness engender by constant physical pain have transform she into a

spirituality just breathe the breath of life and regard she

daughter less as a woman than as a kindre essence from whom no secret

could be hide. at her bedside Olivia thus learn the mystery of birth

and life and death. chiefly the mystery of life, which appeal more to

her ardent maidenhood.



so when at last her mother fade out of existence and Olivia’s vigil be

over, she face a world of change value with a new set of value of

her own. she could not formulate they; but she be acutely conscious

that they be different from those of the good, honest Mr. Trivett and

the dull and honourable Mr. Fenmarch, and that to all the social circle

which these two represent they would be unintelligible. in a way, she

find herself possess of a new calculus in which she trust to solve

the problem which defy the simple arithmetic of the homely folk of

Medlow.



                 *        *        *        *        *



all these memory and vague certainty pass through the girl’s mind

as she sit before the fire in self-examination after her victory, and

conflict with the prosaic and indicatively common-sense argument of

her late adviser. she know that father and brother, all beloved and

revere, would have be staunchly on the side of the Trivetts. on the

other hand, her mother, as she have say to her husband on the edge of a

far, far great adventure, would have say: “go, dear.” of that she have

no doubt. . . . yet it mean cut herself adrift from Medlow and all

its way and all its association. it mean a definite struggle to raise

herself from her father’s second social category to the first. it mean,

therefore, justify herself against odious insinuation on the part of

her scant acquaintance.



and then the youth in her rose insistent. during all these year of

stress and fever which have mark her development from child into woman

she have do nothing but remain immure within the wall familiar from

her babyhood. other girl have go afar, in strange independence, to

vivid scene, to unforgettable adventure, in the service of their

country, in the service of mankind—just as her brother and father have

go—and she have stay there, ineradicable, in that one little tiny

spot. the sick-room, the kitchen, the shop in Old Street, where, in

defiance of Food Controller, she have fight for cream and butter and

egg and english meat so that her mother could live; the sick-room

again, the simple white and green bedroom which mean to her little more

than the sleep of exhaustion; the sick-room once more, with its pathos

of spiritual love and physical repulsion—such have be the iron

environment of her life. sorrow after sorrow, and mourning after

mourning have come, and the little gaiety of the “homely folk” of her

father’s definition have go on without her participation. and her girl

friend of Landsdowne House have either marry rise young tradesman in

distant town, or have find some further scope for their energy at the

end of the Great Adventure and be far away. in the meanwhile other

homely folk whom she do not know have pour into the town. all kind of

people seem to be settle there, anyhow, without rhyme or reason. it

be only when there be not a house to be rent in the neighbourhood

that she understand why.



“you have a comfortable home of your own. why, on earth, don’t you stay

in it?” Mr. Trivett have ask.



but she have stay in it, alone, for the three month since her mother’s

death, wait on the law’s delay; and those three month have be

foretaste enough of the dreary infinite year that would lie before she,

should she remain. she be too young, too full of sap, to face the

blight of sunlessness. she long for the sight and the sound and the

freedom of the great world. what she would do when she get into it, she

do not exactly know. possibly she might meet a fairy prince. if such a

speculation be that of a hussy, why then, she argue, all woman be

hussy from birth. as for be a fool for defy advice on the proper

investment of her money—well, perhaps she be not quite such a fool as

Mr. Trivett imagine. if she do not spend her capital, it would be just

as safe lie on deposit at the bank as invest in stock and share;

safe, for she have lately have wearisome experience of the depreciation

of security. she would not be senselessly extravagant; in fact, with

the sanguineness of youth she hope to be able to live on the interest

on her deposit and the rent of the furnished house. but behind she,

definite, tangible, uninfluence by Stock Exchange fluctuation, would

be her fortune. and then—a contingency which she do not put before Mr.

Trivett and Mr. Fenmarch, for a woman seldom disclose her main argument

to a male adversary—there might come a glorious moment in some now

unconjecturable adventure when it might be essential for she to draw

cheque for dazzle sum which she could put in her pocket and go over

mysterious hill and far away. she stand on the edge of her dull

tableland and gaze wide-eyed at the rolling Land of Romance veil by

gold and purple mist. and in that land, from immemorial time, people

carry their money in bag, into which they dip their hand, as

occasion require, and cast the unmeaning counter at the foot of

poverty or into the lap of greed.



when she sit down to her solitary supper, she have decide that she be

neither hussy nor fool. she hold baffle discourse with Myra, who could

not be entice into enthusiasm over the immediate future. Teasing Myra

have be her joy from infancy. she sketch their career—that of female

Don Quixote and Sancho Panza—that of knight of old in quest of

glorious adventure. she quote, mock heroically:



“the ride abroad redress human wrong.”



“well redress the young London woman which I see the picture of in

the illustrated paper,” say Myra.



Olivia laugh. “you be a dear old blessing, you know.”



“I’m sure of it,” say Myra, with an expressionless face. “anyways,

you’re not go to buy one of they thing when you get to London.”



“I be,” reply Olivia. “and you’ll have to help I put it on.”



“you cann’t help folk put on nothing,” say Myra.



“what do you think you’ll do when you’re really shocked?” ask Olivia.



“I never think what I’ll do,” reply Myra. “it’s waste of time.”



Olivia enjoy her supper.





                               CHAPTER II



it be only when she wait the next morning for her possible tenant,

the Major Olifant of whom Mr. Trivett have speak, and go through the

familiar room to see that they be fit for alien inspection, that she

realize the sacrilege which she be about to commit. every room be

sacred, inhabit by some beloved ghost. the very furniture bore

landmark of the wear and tear of those that be dead. to say nothing

of the bed on which they have sleep, the chair in which they have sit,

which still seem to retain the impress of their form, there persist

a hundred exquisitely memorable triviality. the arm of the oak settle

in the hall still show the ravage of the tooth of Barabbas, the

mongrel bull-terri pup introduce, fifteen year ago, into the house,

by Charles her eld brother; an animal who, from be curse by the

whole family for a pestilential cur, wriggle his way, thank to his

adoration of Charles, into the heart of they all, and die from old age

and perhaps doggy anxiety a few month after Charles have sail for

France. in her father’s study, a small room heterogeneously adorn with

hunt crop and car accessory and stuff trout and a large scale

map of Medlow and neighbourhood and suggestive in no way of a studious

habit, the surface of the knee-hole writing table and the mahogany

having be a careless smoker. there be a legend that the family

cradle, for many year moulder in an outhouse, bear the same

stigmata. the very bathroom be not free of intimate history. in the

plain ground glass—the record of her brother Bobby age twelve, who,

vow vengeance against an unsympathetic visit aunt (soon afterwards

deceased), have the brilliant idea of catapult she through the closed

window while she be have her bath. and there be her mother’s

room. . . .



she could not let all this pass into vulgar hand. the vague plan of

let the house furnish, which have hitherto not be unattractive,

now become monstrously definite. she hate the sacrilegious and

of child to the profanation of these her household god. she go in

search of Myra and find she dust her own prim little bedroom.



“I’m go out. when Major Olifant call, tell he I’ve change my mind

and the house be not to let.”



then she put on hat and coat and go downstairs to take the air of the

sleepy midday High Street. but as she open the front door she run into

a man get out of a two-seater car drive by a chauffeur. he raise

his hat.



“I beg your pardon,” say he, “but be this ‘the tower’?”



“it be,” she reply. “I suppose you’ve—you’ve come with an order to

view from Messrs. Trivett and Gale.”



“quite so,” say he pleasantly. “I have an appointment with Miss Gale.”



“I’m Miss Gale,” say Olivia.



she notice an involuntary twitch of surprise, at once suppress, pass

over his face.



“and my name’s Olifant. Major Olifant.”



she have picture quite a different would-be intruder, a red-faced,

obese, and push fellow. instead, she see a well-breed, spare man of

medium height wear a stained service Burberry the empty left sleeve

of which be pin in front; a man in his middle thirty, with crisp

light brown hair, long, broad forehead characterize by curious bump

over the brow, a very long, straight nose and attractive dark blue eye

which keenly and smilingly hold her without touch of offence.



“I’ve decide not to let the house,” say Olivia.



the smile vanish from his eye. “I’m sorry,” say he stiffly. “I be

give to understand——”



“yes, I know,” she say quickly. her conscience get hold of the

missing arm smite she. “where have you come from?”



“Oxford.”



she gasp. “why, that’s a hundred mile!”



“ninety-four.”



“but you must be perish with cold,” she cry. “do come in and get

warm, at any rate. perhaps I can explain. and your man, too.” she

point. “round that way you’ll find a garage. I’ll send the maid.

please come in, Major Olifant. oh—but you must!”



she enter the house, leave he no option but to follow. to divest

himself of his Burberry he make curious writhing movement with his

shoulder, and swerve aside politely when she offer assistance.



“please don’t worry. I’m all right. I’ve all kind of little stunt of

my own invention.”



and, as he say it, he get clear and throw the mackintosh on the oak

chest. he rub the knuckle of his right hand against the side of his

rough tweed jacket.



“just five minute to get warm and I willn’t trespass far on your

hospitality.”



she show he into the drawing-room, thank goodness there be a showy

wood-fire burning, and go out after Myra.



“I think the house ben’t to be let,” say the latter after receive

many instruction.



“the letting of the house have nothing to do with two cold and hungry man

who have motor here on a raw November morning for hundred of mile on

false pretence.”



she re-enter the drawing-room with a tray bear whisky decanter,

siphon, and glass, which she set on a side table.



“I’m alone in the world now, Major Olifant,” she say, “but I’ve live

nearly all my life with man—my father and two brother——” she feel

that the explanation be essential. “please help yourself.”



he meet her eye, which, though defiant, hold the menace of tear. he

make the vague, most delicate of gesture with his right hand—his

empty sleeve, the air. she move an assent head; then swiftly she

grasp the decanter.



“say when.”



“just that.”



she squirt the siphon.



“so?”



“perfect. a thousand thank.”



he take the glass from she and deferentially await her next movement.

Tricksy memory flash across her mind the picture of the anglo-indian

colonel of her mother’s pathetic little confidence. for a moment or two

she stand confused, flushed, self-conscious, suddenly hate herself for

not know instinctly what to do. in desperation she cry.



“oh, please drink it! you must want it awfully.”



he laugh, make a little bow, and drank.



“now do sit down near the fire. I’m dreadfully sorry,” she continue

journey for nothing. as a matter of fact, I want to let the house and

only change my mind an hour ago.”



“you have live here all your life?” he ask.



“yes.”



“please say no more about it,” say he courteously.



she burst at once into explanation. Father, brother, mother—all the

dear ghost, at the last moment, have hold out their bar hand. he

“there be few house nowadays without ghost. but there might be a

stranger now and then who would have the tact and understanding to win

this be at the end of a talk which have last she know not how long.

the little silence which ensue be break by the shrill clang of the

ormolu clock on the mantelpiece strike one. she spring to her foot.



“one o’clock. why, you must be famish. seven o’clock breakfast at

late. there’ll be something to eat, whatever it be.”



“but, my dear Miss Gale,” cry Major Olifant, rise in protest, “I

couldn’t dream of it—there must be an hotel——”



“there ben’t,” cry Olivia unveraciously, and vanish.



Major Olifant, too late to open the door for she, retrace his step and

stand, back to fire, idly evoke, as a man do, the human purpose

that have go to the making of the room, and he be puzzle. some

delicate spirit have choose the old gold curtain which harmonize with

the cushion on the plain upholstered settee and with the early

chippendale armchair and with the chippendale bookcase fill with odd

and end of good china, old Chelsea, Coalport, a bit or two of Sèvres

he be look round for further evidence of obvious conflict of

individuality, when Myra appear to take he to get rid of the dust

of the journey. when he return to the drawing-room he find Olivia.



“I cann’t help feel an inconscionable intruder,” say he.



“my only concern be that I’ll be able to give you something fit to eat.”



he laugh. “the man who have come out of France and Mesopotamia finikin

in his food be a fraud.”



“still,” she object, “I don’t want to send you back to Mrs. Olifant

rack with indigestion.”



“Mrs. Olifant?” he wear a look of humorous puzzlement.



“I suppose you have a wife and family?”



“good heaven, no!” he cry, with an air of horror. “I’m a bachelor.”



she regard he for a few second, as though from an entirely fresh

point of view.



“but what on earth do a bachelor want with a great big house—with ten

bedroom?”



“have it get ten bedroom?”



“I presume Mr. Trivett send you the particular: ‘Desirable Residence,

stand in own ground, three acre. ten bedroom, three reception

room. Bath H. and C.,’ and so forth?”



“the Bath H. and C. be all I worry about.”



they both laugh. Myra announce luncheon. they go into the

dining-room. by the side of Major Olifant’s plate be a leather case. he

flash on she a look of enquiry, at which the blood rise into her pale

cheek.



“I’ve be interview your man,” she say rather defiantly. “he

produce that from the pocket of the car.”



“you overwhelm I with your kindness, Miss Gale,” say he. “I should

never have have the courage to ask for it.”



the case contain the one-armed man’s patent combination knife and

fork.



“courage be such a funny thing,” say Olivia. “a man will walk up to a

machine-gun in action and knock the gunner out with the butt end of a

rifle; but if he’s sit in a draught in a woman’s drawing-room and

catch his death of cold, he daren’t get up and shut the window. these

be real egg, although they’re camouflage in a chinese scramble. one

faithful hen be still do she one minute day. the other be on

strike.”



she felt curiously exhilarate on this first actual occasion of

assert her independence. only once before have she entertain guest

at her own table, and these be her uncle and aunt from Clapham, the

Edward Gales, who come to her mother’s funeral. they be colourless

supporter of the Church of England to the extent of rent a pew in the

middle gristly bit!” so Olivia feel no call of the blood to Clapham.

and, for all her inherit hospitable impulse, she have be glad when,

she content herself with a bottle of old Corton—her father have be a

judge of full red wine, burgundy and port, and have stock a small but

well-select cellar, and have teach Olivia what be good that a girl

should know concern they.



she watch her guest’s first sip, as her father have be willnot to watch,

and flush with pleasure when he pause, as though take aback,

sniff, sip again, and say:



“either new condition be make I take all sort of goose for swan,

or you’re give I a remarkable wine.”



she burst out radiantly: “how lovely of you to spot it! it’s a Corton,

1887.”



“but forgive I for say so,” he remark. “it’s not a wine you should

spill on any casual tramp. oh, of course,” he protest in anticipation.

“your politeness will assure I that I’m not a casual tramp. but I be.”



“I owe you something for bring you on a fool’s errand. besides, I

want to show you what Todger’s could do when it like!”



“Todger’s be wonderful,” he smile. “and how you could ever have think

of leave Todger’s be more than I can understand.”



“oh, I’m go to leave it, right enough,” she answer. “what on earth

“it’s your home, anyhow.”



“that’s why I don’t like to let it.”



“then why go away from it? if it be not an impertinent question, what

be you go to do?”



she meet his clear blue eye and laugh.



“I’m go out into the world to seek adventure. there!”



“and I,” say he, “want to get out of the world and never have another

adventure as long as I live. I’ve have more than enough for one

“but still,” she retort, conscious of his bearing and vigour and other

conjectured quality, “you cann’t contemplate fossilize here till the

end of time.”



“that’s what I’m literally think of do,” he reply.



she feel the reaction of bitter disappointment. a man like he have no

right to throw up the sponge. the sudden blankness of her face betray

her thought. he smile.



“I say literally, you know. fossilize in the literal and practical

“very fascinating little fossil,” he go on without reference to her

little tiny weeny shell, and the thing once inside they belong to

to the solution of the riddle of existence. I be drag away from they

million of time less important. I’ve get what I think be some new

idea about they, and other thing connect with they—it’s a vast

subject—and so I’m look for a quiet place where I can carry on my

work.”



“that’s awfully interesting,” say Olivia. “but—forgive I—who pay

you for it?”



“possibly mankind two hundred year hence,” he laugh. “but, if I stick

it long enough, they may make I a Fellow of the Royal Society when

I’m—say—seventy-three.”



“I wish you’d tell I some more about these forami—funny little thing

I’ve never hear of,” say Olivia.



but he answer: “no. if once I begin, I would bore you so stiff that

you would curse the hour you allow I to cross your threshold. there

be other thing just as vital as foraminifera. I’ve make my confession,

Miss Gale. now, willn’t you make your? what be you keen on?”



at the direct question, Olivia pass in review the aim and interest

and pleasure of her past young life, and be abash to find they a row

of anæmic little phantom. for year her head have be too full of

duty. she regard he for a moment or two in dismay, then she laugh

in young defiance.



“I suppose I’m keen on real live human being. that’s my starting-point

to the solution of the riddle of existence.”



“we’ll see who get there first,” say he.



when the meal be over, she stand by the door which he hold open for she

and hesitate for a moment.



“I wonder whether you would care to look over the house?”



“I should immensely. but—if you’re not go to let it——”



“you’ll be able, at any rate, to tell Mr. Trivett that he have no

business to send you to such an old rabbit warren,” she reply, with

some demureness.



“I’m at your order,” smile Olifant.



she play cicerone with her little business-like air of dignity, speak

in a learned fashion of water supply, flue, and boiler. Olifant look

wisely at the kitchen range, while Myra stand at impassive attention and

the cook take refuge in the scullery.



“these hole be to put saucepan on, I presume,” say he.



“you’ve hit it exactly,” say Olivia.



they go upstairs. on the threshold of the good bedroom he pause and

cry, in some astonishment: “what an exquisite room!”



“it be my mother’s,” say Olivia. “you can come in. it have a pleasant

view over the garden.”



then Olifant, who have inspect the study, solve the puzzle of the

drawing-room. there the man and woman have compromise. she have suffer

of her delicate scheme. but here her taste reign absolute. it be all

water-colour on the wall, a general impression for curtain and

little row of book in an inlaid stand. with the instinct of a bookish

“may I?”



“of course.” then she add, with a vague longing to impress on a

stranger the wonder and beauty of the spirit that have create these

surrounding: “my mother know they all by heart, I think. naturally she

use to read other thing and I use to read aloud to she—she be

interested in everything till the day of her death—but these book be

part of her life.”



there be: _Marcus Aurelius_, _Lord Herbert of Cherbury_, _the

Imitation of Christ_, _Christina Rossetti_, the almost forget early

Meditation and Premeditated Resolutions_”), _crabbe_ . . . a dozen

volume or so. Olifant pick out one.



“and this, too? the _Pensées de Pascal_?”



“she love it well,” say Olivia.



“it be strange,” say he. “my father spend most of his life on a

remember Pascal as long as I live.”

“he have I christen Blaise.”



“Blaise Olifant,” she repeat critically. she laugh. “he might have

do bad.”



he turn over the page. “there’s one thing here that my father be

always drum into I. yes, here it be. it’s mark in blue pencil.”



“then it must have be drum into I, too,” say Olivia.



“‘_on ne consulte que l’oreille, parce qu’on manque de cœur. La règle

est l’honnêteté._´”



“yes,” she say, with a sigh.



he replace the book. they go in silence out to the landing. after a

few second of embarrassment they turn and descend to the hall.



“I can more than understand, Miss Gale, why you feel you cann’t let the

house. but I’m sorry.”



she weaken, foresee the house empty and desolate, give over to

“it be the idea of a pack of people, the British Family in all its

I’m quite human. I should like to have a place where I can put up a man

servant to dust and so forth—what would you say? not now. think it over

his sympathy and comprehension have win she over. he be big and kind and

brotherly. somehow she feel that her mother would have like he,

accept he without question as one of her own caste, and would have

reflect for a while, then, meet his eye:



“you can have the house, Major Olifant,” she say seriously.



he bow. “I’m sure you will not regret it,” say he. “I ought to remind

you, however,” he add after a pause, “that I may have a stable

companion for a few month. the distinguished fellow I mention. I

wonder whether you’ve hear of Alexis Triona.”



“the man who write _through blood and Snow_?”



“have you read it?”



“of course I have,” cry Olivia. “what do you think I do here all day?

twiddle my thumb or tell my fortune by card?”



“I hope you think it’s a great book,” he say, with a smile.



“an amazing book. and you’re go to bring he to live here? what’s he

“it would take day to tell you.”



“well, compress it into a sort of emergency ration,” say Olivia.



so he sit by her side on the oak settle, near the anthracite stove in

the hall, and tell she what he know of Alexis Triona.





                              CHAPTER III



what Blaise Olifant tell Olivia about his prospective co-inhabitant of

the Towers, and what Rowington, the publisher, and one or two other

know about he, amount to the follow:



one morning a motor-car, have the second-hand air of a hire garage

and unoccupied save for the chauffeur, draw up before the door of a

great London publishing house. the chauffeur step from his seat,

collect a brown-paper package from the interior, and enter.



“can I see a member of the firm?”



the clerk in the enquiry office look surprised. chauffeur offer

manuscript on behalf of their employer be plentiful as blackberry

in September; but chauffeur demand an interview with the august

head of the house be rare as blackberry in March.



“I’m afraid you cann’t do that,” he reply civilly. “if you leave it

here, it will be all right. I’ll give you a receipt which you can take

back.”



“I want to explain,” say the chauffeur.



score of people weekly express the same desire. it be the business

of the clerk to suppress explanation.



“it’s a manuscript to be submit? well, you must tell the author——”



“I be the author,” say the chauffeur.



“oh!” say the clerk, and his subconscious hand push the manuscript a

millimetre forward on the polished mahogany counter.



“the circumstance, you see, be exceptional.”



there be something exceptional in the voice and manner of the

chauffeur, the clerk regard he for the first time as a human being.



“I quite see,” say he; “but the rule of the firm be strict. if you

will leave the manuscript, it will be read. oh, I give you my word of

honour,” he smile. “everything that come in be read. we have a staff

who do nothing else. be your name and address on it?” he begin to untie

the string.



“the name, but not the address.”



on the slip of paper which the clerk push across to he he write:



                       Alexis Triona,

                          c/o John Briggs.

                             3 Cherbury Mews,

                                Surrey Gardens, W.



the clerk scribble an acknowledgment, the chauffeur thrust it into his

pocket, and, drive away, be lose in the traffic of London.



a fortnight afterwards, Alexis Triona, who, together with John Briggs,

as one single and indissoluble chauffeur, inhabit a little room over

the garage in Cherbury Mews, receive a letter to the effect that the

publishing house, be interested in the MS. “_through blood and

Snow_,” which he have kindly submit, would be glad if he would call,

with a view to publication. the result be a second visit on the part of

the chauffeur to the great firm. the clerk welcome he with a bland

smile, and show he into a comfortably furnish room whose thick

Turkey carpet signify the noiseless mystery of many discreet decade,

and where a benevolent middle-aged man in gold spectacle stand with his

back to the chimney-piece. he advance with outstretched hand to meet

the author.



“Mr. Triona? I’m glad to meet you. willn’t you sit down?”



he motion to a chair by the tidy writing table, where he sit and

pull forward the manuscript, which have be place there in readiness

for the interview. he say pleasantly:



“well. let we get to business at once. we should like to publish your

book.”



the slight quivering of sensitive nostril alone betray the author’s

emotion.



“I’m glad,” he reply. “I think it’s worth publish.”



Mr. Rowington tap the MS. in front of he with his forefinger. “be

these your own personal experience?”



“they be,” say the chauffeur.



“excuse my question you,” say the publisher. “not that it would

greatly matter. but one like to know. we should be incline to publish

it, either as a work of fiction or a work of fact; but the handling of

it—the method of publicity—would be different. of course, you see,” he

go on benevolently, “a thing may be absolutely true in essence, like

lot of the brilliant little war story that have be write the past

few year, but not true in the actual historical sense. now, your book

would have more value if we could say that it be true in this actual

historical sense, if we could say that it’s an authentic record of

personal experience.”



“you can say that,” answer Triona quietly.



the publisher lean back in his chair.



“how a man could have go through what you have and remain sane

pass understanding.”



for the first time the young man’s set feature relax into a smile.



“I shouldn’t like to swear that I be sane,” say he.



“I’ve hear ex-prisoner say,” Mr. Rowington remark, “that six month’

solitary confinement under such conditions”—he pat the

manuscript—“is as much as the human reason can stand.”



“as soon as hunt and kill vermin cease to be a passionate

interest in life,” say Triona.



they converse for a while. stimulate by the publisher’s question,

Triona supplement detail in the book, describe his final adventure,

his landing penniless in London, his search for work. at last, say he,

he have find a situation as chauffeur in the garage of a motor-hire

company. the publisher glance at the slip pin to the cover of the

manuscript.



“and John Briggs?”



“a pseudonym. Briggs be my mother’s name. I be english on both side,

though my great-grandfather’s people be maltese. my father, however,

be a naturalized Russian. I’ve mention it in the book.”



“quite so,” say the publisher. “I only want to get thing clear. and

now as to term. have you any suggestion?”



afterwards, Alexis Triona confess to a wild impulse to ask for a

hundred pound—outright sale—and to a sudden lack of audacity which

keep he silent. the term which the publisher propose, when the

royalty system and the probability of such a book’s profit be

an advance in respect of royalty exceed the hundred pound of his

crazy prompting, his heart thump until it become an all but

intolerable pain.



“do you think,” he ask, amazed that his work should have such market

value, “that I could earn my living by write?”



“undoubtedly.” the publisher beam on the new author. “you have the

matter, you have the gift, the style, the humour, the touch. I’m sure I

could place thing for you. indeed, it would be to our common advantage,

pende publication. only, of course, you mustn’t use any of the matter

in the book. you quite understand?”



Alexis Triona understand. he go away dance on air. write? his brain

seethe with idea. that the write expression of they should open the

gate of Fortune be a new conception. he have put together the glowing,

vivid book impel by strange, unknown force. it be, as he have

confidently declare, worth publishing. but the possible reward be

beyond his dream. and he could see more vision. . . .



so he go back to his garage and drive idle people to dinner and

theatre, and in his scanty leisure write strange romance of love and

war in Circassia and Tartary, and, through the agency of the powerful

be publish, and, justify the confidence of the great firm, blaze

for, in their hundred, they have come to judgment. but know very

                 *        *        *        *        *



it be at Oxford, whither the sudden wave of fame have drift he, that

“my dear lady,” say he, “there be a theory in the United States

immigrant buy it to familiarize themselves with the negro question.

russian literature have just as much to do with the Russia of to-day.

why should these folk not be content with a plain human story of almost

he like the man. he like the boyish, clean-shaven face, the broad

careless air of the slim young man of no account; he like the courteous

patience of his manner. he understand his little nervous trick of

pluck at his lip.



in the drawing-room after dinner Mrs. Head of College say to he:



“a most interesting man—but I do wish he would look you in the face

great cinematograph film which have hold all London breathless.

“the boy’s a magician.”



Blaise Olifant smile. “the boy’s a man,” say he.



                 *        *        *        *        *



Chance throw they together a while later in London. there they meet

member of a high chapter.



“London be wonderful,” say Triona one day. “London’s appreciation of

precious novel of mine?”



Blaise Olifant reflect for a moment.



“I myself be look for a sort of hermitage. in fact, I’ve hear of one

natural life. so there would be room for a guest. if you would care to

come and stay with I, wherever I pitch my comfortable tent, and carry

on your job while I carry on mine, you would be more than welcome.”



“my dear fellow,” cry Triona, impulsively thrust out both hand to

be shake, “this be unheard-of generosity. it mean my soul’s salvation.

only the horrible dread of loneliness—you know the old solitary

out-of-the-way place—say in Cornwall. I’ve shrink from it. but London

be different. in my chauffeur’s day it be different. I have always

associate, fare, the multitudinous sight and sound of the vast city.

but solitude in a village! frankly, I funk it. I’ve live so much

alone that now I must talk. if I don’t talk I should go mad. or rather

I must feel that I can talk if I want to. I keep hold of myself,

however. if I bore you with my loquacity you wouldn’t have make I your

delightful proposal.”



“well, you’ll come, if I can get the right kind of house?”



“with all the gratitude in life,” cry Triona, his eye sparkle. “but

not as your guest. some daily, weekly, monthly arrangement, so that we

shall both be free—you to kick I out—I to go——”



“just as you like,” laugh Olifant. “I only should be pleased to have

your company.”



“and God know,” cry Triona, “what yours would be to I.”





                               CHAPTER IV



JOHN FREKE be one of the most highly respected man in Medlow. a great

leader in municipal affair, he have twice be Mayor of the town and be

chairman of the local hospital, President of club and association

innumerable, and hold Provincial Masonic rank. but as John Freke

that she prosper. the good of relation apparently exist between

she enter into possession a fortnight after her interview with Blaise

“why should thing so dear be so dismal?”

and then practical memory administer a prosaic jog to her aspiring

she radiate health and vigour. also proclaim sex defiant, vaguely

from the illustrate paper she have become aware of the existence of

“all sort. some be of the very good—the backbone of the nation. they

“be you ever go to bed?”



“I suppose I must,” say Olivia.

                               CHAPTER V



the Odyssey or the Argonautic, or whatever you like to call the epic of

the first wild adventure of a young woman into the Infinite of Clothes,

have yet to be write. it would need not only a poet, but a master of

psychology, to record the myriad vibration of the soul as it react to

temptation, yielding, tremulous thrill of the flesh, exquisite

apprehension, flutter joy, and each last voluptuous plenitude of

content. it be an adventure which absorb every faculty of the will;

which ignore hunger and thirst, weariness of limb and ache of head;

which make the day a dream of reality and the night the reality of a

dream. hardened woman of the world with frock-wear mind be catch at

time by the lure of the adventure, even when it be a question of a

dress or two and a poor half a dozen hat. but how manifold more potent

the spell in the case of one who start with her young body in

nymph-like innocence and be call upon to clothe it again and again in

infinite variety, from toe to head, from innermost secret daintiness to

outward splendour of bravery!



such a record would explain Olivia, not only to the world, but to

herself during that first fortnight in London. her hour could be

reckon by gasp of wonder. she lose count of time, of money, of human

value. thing that have never before enter into her philosophy, such

as the subtle shade of silk stocking which would make or mar a costume,

loom paramount in importance. the after-use scarcely occur to she.

sufficient for the day be the chiffon thereof; also the gradual

transformation of herself from the prim slip of a girl with just the

pretension (in her own mind) to good look, into a radiant and somewhat

distinguish dark-haired little personage.



her shrinking, her argument with Lydia Dawlish, her defeat, go all

into the melting-pot of her delight. “no bath salt, my dear?” cry

Lydia. “whoever hear of a woman not use bath salt?” so bath salt

be order. and—horrified: “my dear, you don’t mean to say you wash

your face in soap and water. what will become of your skin?” so Olivia

be put under the order of a West End specialist, who stock she

dressing-table with delectable cream and oil. it be all so new, so

unheard of, so wonderful to the girl, an experience worth the living

through, even though all thousand at deposit at the bank should vanish

at the end of it. merely to sit in a sensuously furnish room and have

beautiful woman parade before she, clothe in dream of loveliness—any one

of which be her for a scribble on a bit of pink paper—evoke within

her strange and almost spiritual emotion. Medlow be countless league

away; this transcend the London even of her most foolish vision.



Afterwards Olivia, when, sense of value be restore she look back

on this phantasmagoria of dressmaker, milliner, lingerie maker and

furrier, say to Lydia Dawlish:



“it’s funny, but the fact that there might be a man or so in the world

never enter my head.”



and the wise Lydia answer: “you be too busy turn yourself into a

woman.”



twice or thrice during this chrysali period she steal out of night

with Myra to the dress circle of a theatre, where, besides ingenuous joy

in the drama, she find unconfessed consolation in the company of homely

folk like herself—girl in clean blouse or simple little frock like

her own, and young man either in well-wear khaki or morning dress. on

these occasion she wonder very much what she be about to do in the

other galley—that of the expensively furred and jewelled haughtiness

and impudence whom she shoulder in the vestibule crush and whom she

see drive away in luxurious limousine. these flash personality

frighten she with their imply suggestion of world beyond her ken.

one woman make especial impression on she—a woman tall, serene, with a

clear-cut face, vaguely familiar, and a beautiful voice; she overhear a

commonplace phrase or two address to the escort man. she brush

Olivia’s arm and turn with a smile and a word of gracious apology and

pass on. Olivia catch a whisper behind she. “that’s the Marchioness

of Aintree. ben’t she lovely?” but she do not need to be tell that she

have be in contact with a great lady. and she go home doubt

exceedingly whether, for all her flourish of social trumpet, Lydia

Dawlish’s galley be that of Lady Aintree.



Criticism of Lydia, however, she put behind she as ingratitude, for

Lydia make up royally for past negligence. time and energy that ought to

have be devote to Lydia, Ltd., be divert to the creation of

Olivia.



“I don’t know why you’re so good to I,” she would say.



and the other, with a little mocking smile round her lip: “it’s worth

it. I’m give myself a new experience.”



                 *        *        *        *        *



the first occasion on which she go out into the great world be that

of Sydney Rooke’s party. she know that her low-cut, sleeveless,

short-skirt gown of old gold tissue have material existence, but she

feel herself half-ashamedly, half-deliciously clothe in nothing but a

bodily sensation. a faint blush linger in her cheek all the evening.

Lydia, call for she in Rooke’s car, which have be place at her

disposal, hold she at arm’s length in sincere and noble admiration,

move by the artist’s joy in behold the finished product of his toil,

and embrace she fondly. then she survey she again, from the little

gold brocade slipper to the diamond butterfly (one of her mother’s bit

of jewellery) in her dark wavy hair.



“you’re the dainty elf in London,” she cry.



to the dinner at the Savoy Sydney Rooke have invite a white-moustache

soldier, Major-General Wigram, whose blue undress uniform, to the

bedazzlement of Olivia, gleam with four long row of multi-coloured

ribbon; a vivacious middle-aged woman, Mrs. Fane Sylvester, who write

novel, play, book of travel, and fashion article in a weekly

periodical—Olivia learn all this in their first five-minute converse

in the lounge; Sir Paul and Lady Barraclough, he a young baronet whose

civilian evening dress could not proclaim hard-won distinction, she a

pretty, fair, fragile creature, both of they obviously react joyously

to relaxation of tension; and, last, the Vicomte de Mauregard, of the

French Embassy, young, good looking, who speak polished English with a

faultless accent. it be, socially, as correct a little party as the

brooding, innocent spirit of Mrs. Gale could have desire for she

about-to-be prodigal daughter. Olivia sit between her host and

Mauregard. on her host’s right be Lady Barraclough; then the General,

then Lydia, then Sir Paul, face Rooke at the round table, then Mrs.

Fane Sylvester, who be Mauregard’s left-hand neighbour. they be by

the terrace window, far from what Olivia, with her fresh mind play

on social phenomenon, hold then and ever afterwards, most rightly, to be

the madden and human intercourse-destroy band.



not that her first entrance down the impose broad staircase, into the

lounge fill with mirifically vesture fellow-creature, to the

accompaniment of a clash rag-time imbecility, do not set all her

young nerve vibrate to the point of delicious agony. it be like a

mad fanfare herald her advent in a new world. but soon she find that

the blare of the idiot music deaden all other sense. before her eye

sway black-and-white thing whom at the back of her mind she

recognize as man, and various form all stark flesh, flash jewel

and a maze of colour, whom she know to be woman. the gathering group of

her own party seem but figure of a dream. her unaccustomed ear could

not catch a word of the conventional gambit of conversation open, on

introduction, by her fellow guest. it be only when they pass between

the table of the great restaurant and the horrible noise of the

negroid, syncopated parody of tune grow fainter and fainter, and they

reach the peace of the terrace side, that the madden clatter fade

from her ear and consciousness of her surrounding return.



then she surrender herself to huge enjoyment. both her neighbour have

be all over the world and see all sort and condition of man. they

be vividly aware of current event. Pride would not allow she to

betray the fact that often they speak of matter far beyond her

experience of man and thing. under their stimulus she begin to regain

the self that, for the past fortnight, the cardboard box of London have

snow under.



“it’s no use ask I,” she say to Mauregard, “whether I’ve be to

Monte Carlo or Madagascar or Madame Tussaud’s, for I haven’t. I haven’t

be anywhere. I’ve somehow exist at the back of Nowhere, and to-night

I’ve come to life.”



“but where do you come from? the sea foam? Venus Anadyomene?”



“no, I’m of the other kind. I come from far inland. I believe they call

it Shropshire. that oughtn’t to convey anything to you.”



“indeed it do!” cry Mauregard. “be I not at school at Shrewsbury?”



“no?”



“but yes. three year. so I’m Shropshire, too.”



“that’s delightful,” she remark; “but it do away with my little

mystery of Nowhere.”



“no, no,” he protest, with a laugh. he be a fair, bright-eyed boy

with a little curl-up moustache which give he the air of a cherub

playfully disguised. “it be the county of mystery. don’t your poet

say:



                    ‘once in the wind of morning

                      I range the thymy wold;

                    the world-wide air be azure

                      and all the brooks run gold.’”



“that’s from _a Shropshire Lad_,” cry Olivia.



“of course. so why shouldn’t you have come from the wind of morning, the

azure world-wide air or the golden brook?”



“that’s beautiful of you,” say Olivia. “well, why shouldn’t I? it’s

more romantic and imaginative than the commonplace old sea. the sea have

be overdone. I use to look at it once a year, and, now I come to

think of it, it always seem to be self-conscious, try to live up to

its reputation. but ‘the wind of the morning——’ anyhow, here I be.”



“blow to London by the wind of a Shropshire morning.”



Olivia’s spirit dance in the talk. with his national touch on the

light emotion, Mauregard draw from she an exposition of the Dryad’s

sensation on sudden confrontation with modern life. to talk well be a

great gift; to compel other to talk well be a great; and the latter

gift be Mauregard’s. Olivia put food into her mouth, but whether it be

fish or flesh or fowl she know not. when her host break the spell by an

announcement in her ear that he have a couple of box for “jazz-jazz,”

she become aware that she be eat partridge.



Mr. Sydney Rooke talk of woman’s clothe, of which he have an expert

knowledge. Lady Barraclough chime in. Olivia, fresh from the welter,

speak as one in authority. now and again she catch Lydia’s eye across

the table and receive an approve nod. the elderly General regard

she with amused admiration. she begin to taste the first-fruit of

social success. she drive in a taxi to the theatre with the Barracloughs

and Mrs. Fane Sylvester and sit with they in a box during the first act

of the gay revue. for the second act there be a change of company and

she find herself next to the General. he have serve in India and be

familiar with the name of her mother’s people. what anglo-indian be

not? long ago he have meet an uncle of her; dead, poor chap. this social

placing give she a throb of pleasure, set she, at least, in a

stranger’s eye, in her mother’s sphere. the performance over, they

part great friend.



General Wigram and Mrs. Fane Sylvester excuse themselves from go on

to Percy’s, the other crowd into Sydney Rooke’s limousine. the crash

of jazz music welcome they. already a few couple be dance; other
