point out to Olivia’s wonder eye the star of the theatrical

firmament who condescend to walk the parquet floor of the famous night

club. he also indicate here and there a perfectly attired youth as a

professional dancer.



“on the stage?”



he explain that they have their professional partner and give

exhibition dance, show the new step. they also give private

lesson. it be the way they make their living. Olivia knit a

perplex brow.



“it don’t seem a very noble profession for a young man.”



Sydney Rooke shrug his shoulder politely.



“I’m with you a thousand time, my dear Miss Gale. the parasite, _per

se_, ben’t a noble object. but what would you have? the noble thing of

the past few year come to an end a short while ago, and, if I can read

the time, reaction have already begin. in six month’ time the noble

fellow will be a hopeless anachronism.”



“do you mean,” ask Olivia, “that all the young man will be rotten?”



he smile. “how direct you be! disconcert, if I may say so. so

positive; while I be approach the matter from the negative side.

there’ll be a universal loss of ideal.”



Olivia protest. “the young man have before he the reconstruction of

the world.”



“oh no,” say Rooke. “he have do his bit. he expect other people to

carry out the reconstruct business for he. all he care about be to

find a couple of sixpence to jingle together in his pocket.”



“and have these young man who devote their life to foxtrot do

their bit?”



he beg the question. “pray be guide by my prophecy, Miss Gale. next

year you mustn’t mention war to ear polite. these young man be alive.

they thank God for it. let you and I do likewise.”



this little supper-table talk be the only cloud on a radiant night. the

Vicomte de Mauregard take she to dance. at first she feel awkward,

know only the simple step of five year ago. but instinct soon

guide she, and for two hour she dance and dance in an unthinking

ecstasy. the clattering and unmeaning din which have daze she on her

entrance to the Savoy be now pregnant with physical significance. the

tearing of the string, the clashing of the cymbal, the barbaric

thumping of the drum, the sudden raucous scream from negro throat, set

vibrate within her responsive chord of an atavistic savagery. when

each nerve-tear cacophony come to its abrupt end, she join

breathlessly with the suddenly halt crow in eager clapping for the

encore. and then, when the blood-stir string and cymbal crash

out, overpower the staccato of hand beat hand, she surrender

herself with an indrawn sigh of content to her partner’s arm—to the

rhythm, to the movement, to the mere bodily guidance, half conscious of

the proud flexibility of her frame under the man’s firm clasp, to

something, she know not what, far remote from previous experience.

strange, too, the personality of the man do not matter. Paul

Barraclough, Sydney Rooke, Mauregard, she dance with they all in turn.

in her pulsate happiness she mix they all up together, so that a

flash glance, liable to be misinterpret, proceed from a mere

impulse of identification. now and then, in the swimming throng of man

and woman, and the intoxication of pass raiment impregnate with

scent and cigarette smoke, she exchange an absent smile with Lydia and

Lady Barraclough. otherwise she scarcely realize their existence. she

be lead pant by Mauregard to a supper table while he go in search

of refreshment. he return with a waiter, apologize for the

abomination of iced ginger ale and curl orange peel, which be all

that the law of the land allow he to offer. horse’s neck, it be

call. she laugh, delight with the name, and, after drinking,

laugh again, delight with the cool liquid so tingle on her palate.



“it’s a drink for the god,” she declare.



“if you offer it, the unfortunate Bacchus would drink it without a

murmur.”



“do you really think it’s so awful?”



“_Mon Dieu!_” reply the young Frenchman.



then Lydia come up with a dark-eye, good-look boy in tow, whom she

introduce, as Mr. Bobbie Quinton and Olivia be surprised to recognize

as one of the professional. she accept, however, his invitation to

dance and go off on his arm. she find he a boy of charming manner

and agreeable voice, and in the lightness and certainty of his dancing

he far outclass her other partner. he suggest new step. she try

and blunder. she excuse herself.



“this be the first time I’ve dance for four year.”



“it don’t matter,” say he. “you’re a bear dancer. you only need a few

lesson to bring you up to date. what I find in so many of the woman I

teach be that they not only don’t begin to understand what they’re

try to do, but that they never try to understand. you, on the other

hand, have it instinctively. but, of course, you cann’t learn step in a

place like this.”



“I wonder if you could give I some lesson?”



“with all the pleasure in life, Miss Gale,” reply Mr. Bobbie Quinton

promptly.



                 *        *        *        *        *



about two o’clock in the morning Sydney Rooke and Lydia deposit Olivia

at the front door of Victoria Mansions. Rooke stand hat in hand as she

enter.



“I hope you’ve not be too bored by our little evening.”



“Bored! it have be just one heaven after another opening out before

I.”



“but not the seventh. if only I could have provide that!”



“I’ll find it in the happy and sound night’s rest I ever have,”

say Olivia.





                               CHAPTER VI



this be life; magical, undreame of in her wild Medlow dream. and

thank to Lydia, she have plunge into it headlong, after a mere

fortnight’s probation. there have be no disillusion. she have plunge

and emerge into her kingdom. London conspire to strew her path with

rose. the Barracloughs invite she to a dinner party at their home in

Kensington. General Wigram offer her dinner and theatre and convene

to meet she an old indian crony, General Philimore, and his young

daughter, Janet. Philimore have know her grandfather, Bagshawe of the

Guides, when he be a subaltern, infinite age ago. the world be a

small place, after all. Olivia, care little for grandfather beyond

their posthumous social guarantee, find youth’s real sympathy in Janet,

who hold open for she their flat in Maida Vale. Young Mauregard, after

their first lunch together at the Carlton, seem prepared to provide

she with free meal and amusement for the rest of time. it be true he

be madly in love with a russian dancer, whose eccentric way and

abominable treatment of he form the staple of the conversation which

he pour into her very interested and compassionate ear. and, last,

Bobbie Quinton give her dancing lesson at the flat at the rate of a

guinea apiece.



Christmas cause a break in these social activity. Lydia take she off

to Brighton, where, meet various acquaintance of her chaperone and

make other of her own, she motor and dance and dance and motor,

and in the pursuit of these delight discover, with a fearful joy,

that she could hold she own in the immemorial conflict of sex. Sydney

Rooke, having drive down for the day, occasionally flash through the

hotel, the eternal smile of youth on his dark, line face and his

gesture unceasingly polite. as he pass, the heaven open and rain

champagne and box of chocolate and hot-house fruit and flower and

embroider handbag, and once, a pekinese dog for Lydia. once again, an

automobile seem about to fall, but at Lydia’s protest it melt in

the ether.



“a dog and a rose and a glass of wine,” say she, “be a woman’s due for

amuse a man. but a motor-car be profiteer. besides, it’s bind to

drive you somewhere in the end—either to the flat of shame or the

country house of married respectability: it only depend on who be at

the wheel.”



“I see,” say Olivia. but she don’t. Sydney Rooke be a mystery; and

Lydia’s attitude towards he be more than her inexperience could

understand.



still, there she be in the pleasant galley and she do not question

what she be do in it. in a dim way she regard it as the inevitable

rescue vessel after universal shipwreck. her eye be blind by its

glitter and her ear deafen by its music to the welter of the unsalved

world.



just before New Year she receive a letter from Bobby Quinton. it begin:

“Dearest of Ladies.” never before having be thus apostrophize, she

think it peculiarly graceful and original. the writing be refine and

exquisitely clear. to his dearest of lady the young man bewail she

absence; life be dreary without her friendship and encouragement; all

this Christmastide he be the lonely thing on earth; he suggest

that there be no one to love he—no mother or sister to whom he could

apply for comfort; this terrible night life to which he, poor

demobilize soldier of fortune, be condemn in order to earn his

bread, weigh upon his spirit and affect his health; he envy his

dearest of lady’ sojourn by the invigorate sea; he long for the

taste of it; but such health-restore rapture he give she, in the most

delicate way, to understand, be for fairy princess and not for the

impecunious demobbed; he count the day till her return and pray she

to bring back a whiff of ozone on her garment to revive the ever

faithful one who have the temerity to try to teach she to dance.



a most piteous epistle. Bobby Quinton, by his ingratiate way and his

deference and his wit, have efface her original conception of the type

of young man who dance at night club for their living. she like he.

he seem so young and she, through her long companionship with sorrow,

so old in comparison; he seem so foolish and impossible, and she so

wise; to she, remember the helpless dependence of her father and

brother, he seem (motherless and sisterless as he be) lose in a

hostile world. besides, he be not a nameless adventurer. his father

(long since decease) have be a Colonial Governor. he have be to one

of the great public school. in short, he have the birth and breeding of

a gentleman. she slip on a dressing-gown and go with the letter to

Lydia, full of maternal purpose.



it be nine o’clock in the morning. their room have a communicating

door. she find Lydia daintily attire in boudoir cap and

dressing-jacket, have breakfast in bed.



“the poor boy’s die for a breath of sea air. it would do he an

enormous amount of good. do you think we—of course, it really would be

I—but it would be well if it appear to be a joint affair—do you

think we could, without offend he, ask he to come down here for a

couple of day as our guest?”



Lydia, who have read the letter with a smile round her lip, reply

drily:



“as far as Bobby be concern—I really think we could.”



“and as far as we be concern,” flash Olivia, “why should the silly

fact of be a woman prevent we from help a lame dog over a stile?”



“a he-dog,” say Lydia.



“what do it matter?” Olivia ask stoutly.



Lydia laugh in her half-cynical, tolerant way.



“do as you like, dear. I don’t mind. you’re out for experience, not I.

I’d only have you remark that our he-dog friend Bobby be sit up and

beg for the invitation——”



“oh! ah!” cry Olivia, with a fling of her arm, “you’re horrid!”



“not a bit,” smile Lydia. “I face fact, as you’ll have to do, if you

want to find comfort in this matter-of-fact world. have your Bobby down

by all mean. only keep your eye on he.”



“he’s not my Bobby,” say Olivia indignantly.



“our Bobby, then,” say Lydia, with good-natured indulgence.



so Olivia, with the little palpitation of the heart attendant on

consciousness of adventurous and (in Medlow eye, preposterous)

well-doing, write to Bobby Quinton a letter whose gracious delicacy

would not have wound the susceptibility of a needy Hidalgo or an

impoverished Highland chieftain, and receive in reply a telegram of

eager acceptance.



Bobby appear immaculately vesture, his heart overflow with

gratitude at the amazing sweetness of his two dear lady. never have man

be bless with such fairy godmother. by the fresh frankness of his

appreciation of their hospitality he disarm criticism. a young son

hang on to the court of Louis XIII never receive purse of gold from

his lady love with less embarrassed grace. he devote himself to their

service. he have the art of tactful effacement, and of appearance at the

exact moment of welcome. he enliven their meal with chatter and a

boyish brightness that pass for wit.



to Olivia, the dear of his dear lady, he confide the pathetic

history of his life. a sunny, shelter corner of the Pier, both sit

side by side well wrap in fur, conduce to intimacy. how a young man

in such a precarious financial position could afford to wear a fur-line

coat with a new astrachan collar it do not strike Olivia to enquire.

that he, like herself, be warm on that sun-fill morning, with the sea

dancing and sparkle away beyond they, and human type around they

exude the prosperity of peace, seem sufficient for the comfortable

hour. he speak of his early year of ease, of his modest patrimony

come to an end soon after the war break out; of his commission in a

yeomanry regiment; of his heart-break as the month go on and the

chance of the regiment be send to the front grow less and less; of

his exchange into a regiment of the line; of the rotten heart that give

out after a month in France; of his grief at be invalid out of the

army and his struggle and anxiety when he return to civil life,

brand as physically unfit. he have try the stage, musical comedy,

male youth in the manless chorus be eagerly welcome; then, after a

little training, he find he have the dancer’s gift. “so one thing lead to

another,” say he, “and that’s my history.”



“but surely,” say Olivia, “all this dancing and these late hour must

be very bad for your heart.”



he smile sadly. “what do it matter? I’m no use to anybody, and nobody

care whether I’m dead or alive.”



Olivia protest warmly. “the world be cry out for young man of

three-and-twenty. you could be useful in a million way.”



“not a crock like I.”



“you could go into an office.”



“yes. in at one door and out of another. hopeless.”



he draw from a slim gold case a turkish cigarette—Olivia, minutely

hospitable, have put a box of a hundred in his room—and tap it

thoughtfully.



“after all, which be well—to carry on with life like a worm—which

anyhow perisheth, as the Bible tell we—or to go out like a butterfly,

with a bit of a swagger?”



“but you mustn’t talk of go out,” cry Olivia. “it’s indecent.”



Bobby light his cigarette. “who would care?”



“I, for one,” she reply.



her health and sanity revolt against morbid idea. he stretch out

his hand, and, with the tip of his finger, touch her coat, and he

bent his dark brown eye upon she.



“would you really?” he murmur.



she flush, feel angry she scarce know why, and put herself swiftly on

the defensive.



“I would care for the life of any young man. after a million kill it’s

precious—and every decent girl would care the same as I.”



“you’re wonderful!” he remark.



“I’m common sense incarnate,” say Olivia.



“you be. you’re right. you’re right a thousand time,” he reply.

“I’ll always remember what you have say to I this morning.”



at his surrender she disarm. a corpulent, opulent couple pass they

by, the lady wear a cheap feather hat and a rope of pearl outside

a Kolinsky coat, the gentleman display on an ungloved right hand,

which maintain in his mouth a gigantic cigar, an enormous ruby set in

a garden border of diamond.



“at any rate,” say Bobby, “I’m not as some other man be.”



so they laugh and discuss the profiteer and walk back to the

hotel for lunch with the sharpen appetite of twenty.



when Bobby Quinton leave they, Olivia reproach herself for lack of

sympathy. the boy have do his good. a rotten, and crocky heart, who be

she to despise? but for circumstance he might have do heroic thing.

perhaps in his defiance of physical disability he be do a heroic

thing even now. still. . . . to Lydia, in an ironically tease mood,

she declare:



“when I do fall in love, it’s not go to be with any one like Bobby

Quinton. I want a man—there would be a devil of a row, of course, if he

try—but one capable of beat I.”



“Bobby would do that, right enough, if you give he the chance,” say

Lydia.



Olivia reflect for a while. “why have you get your knife into he like

that?” she ask abruptly.



“I haven’t, my dear child. if I have, do you think I would have allow

he to come down? I live and let live. by let live, I live very

comfortably and manage, with moderate mean, to have a very good time.”



Olivia, already dress for dinner, look down on the easy, creamy,

handsome, kimono-clothe woman, curl up like a vast Angora cat on the

hotel bedroom sofa, and once more be dimly conscious of a doubt whether

the galley of Lydia Dawlish be the one for her mother’s daughter to row

in.



still, _vogue la galère_. when she return to London there be little

else to do. eat and dancing fill many of her day and night. she

try to recapture the pleasure of book which have be all her

recreation for year; but, although her life be not a continuous whirl

of engagement—for it require a great vogue as a pretty and

unattached young woman than Olivia possess to be book for fourteen

meal and seven evening every week of the year—she find little time

for solitary intelligent occupation. if she be at a loose end, Lydia’s

hat shop provide an agreeable pastime. or, as a thousand little odd

and end of dress demand attention, there be always a sensuous hour

or two to be spend at Pacotille’s and Luquin’s or Deville’s. tea

companion seldom fail. when she have no evening engagement she be

glad to get to bed, soon after the dinner in the downstairs restaurant,

and to sleep the sleep of untroubled youth. and all the time the spell

of London still hold her captive. to walk the crowded street, to join

the feminine crush before the plate-glass window of great shop, to

watch the strange bird in the ornamental water in St. James’s Park, to

wander about the Abbey and the Temple Gardens, to enter on the moment’s

impulse a Bond Street picture gallery or a cinema—all be a matter of

young joy and thrill. she even spend a reckless and rapturous afternoon

at Madame Tussaud’s. sometimes Janet Philimore accompany she on these

excursion round the monument of London. Janet, who have mild

antiquarian taste and a proletarian knowledge of London traffic, take

vision of myriad Bloomsbury lodging-house at whose door he have

“_ce que femme veut, Mauregard le veut._”

                              CHAPTER VII



OLIVIA sit by her little table, dispense tea and accept homage with

“it be a trick of environment,” he say, with a smile in his dark blue

the feminine in she catch the admiration behind they and delightedly

the unconscious owner have only to turn his back for a second. then, of

course, you hide in a wood and have an orgy.”

he throw out his hand. “ah! that be the secret. keep it to yourself.

she dine in her little quiet corner of the restaurant, and after dinner

                              CHAPTER VIII



the taste of Alexis Triona be not such as to lead he into

extravagant live on the fruit of his literary success. to quality of

food he be indifferent; wine he neither understand nor care for; in

the use of other form of alcohol he be abstemious; unlike most man

breed in Russia he smoke moderately, prefer the cigarette he roll

himself from Virginia tobacco to the more expensive turkish or egyptian

brand. his attire be simple. he would rather walk than be drive; and

he regard his back-bedroom at the top of the Vanloo Hotel as a

luxurious habitation.



he have break away from the easeful life at Medlow because, as he

explain to Blaise Olifant, it frighten he.



“I’m up against nothing here,” say he.

who can say that, in after year, these twain, when they shall have

grow old and have go through whatever furnace Fate—either personal

man do not lay thing up in lavender as woman do.

“then you’re lucky,” retort Myra, and fade away into her kitchen.

                               CHAPTER IX



HERE, all in a rush of twenty-four hour, be a glut of incident for a

                 *        *        *        *        *



that be the end of the argument. Olivia recognize in Myra every useful

                               CHAPTER x



he bring great news. not only have his publisher think well of the

                               CHAPTER XI



SOMEWHERE on the South Coast, screen from the vulgar by the trap of a

huge watering-place, be a long, thin, sandy promontory stick out to

sea, like an innocent rib of wilderness. here there be no fun of the

fair, because there be no fair to provide the fun. there be no tavern,

no boarding-house, no lodging. one exclusive little hotel rule the

extreme tip of the tongue of land in consort with the miniature jetty

and quay by which, in late exciting time, strange craft be moor,

fly the white ensign and hoar with North Sea brine and deadly

secret. the rest of the spit be pepper with a score of little shy

house, each try to hide itself from its neighbour, in the privacy

of its own sandpit. if your house be on the more desirable side of it,

you can look out over the vastness of the sea with the exhilarate

certainty (if your temperament may thereby be exhilarate) that there be

nothing but blue water between you and the coast of Africa. if your

house be, less fortunately, on the other side, your view command a

spacious isle-stud harbour fringe by distant blue and mysterious

hill. but it be give to any one to walk out of the back of his little

hermitage, and, stand in the divide road, to enjoy, in half a

minute, both aspect at once. it be call esoterically by its

frequenter “the point,” so that the profane, map-searching, may not

discover its whereabouts.



just high enough to be under the lee of a sand-hill, with its front

window and veranda stare at the african coast, some thousand mile

away, stand the tiny, most fragile and most absurd of the

habitation. its name be “Quien Sabe,” suggestive of an imaginative

abandonment of search after nomenclature by the original proprietor.



“a house call ‘Quien Sabe’——” say Alexis.



“be the house for we,” cry Olivia, aglow.



they take it at once, without question. it ben’t as if it be an

uncertain sort of place, like “Normanhurst,” or “Sea View.” the name

proclaim frankly the certainty of venturesomeness. and Alexis Triona,

moonlight, laugh the laugh of deep happiness at their childish

inspiration. he roll, lick and light the final cigarette. Tobacco be

good. well be this August night of velvet and diamond. below, the

little stone groin shine like onyx. the lazy surf of ebb-tide far away

on the sand of a tiny bay glimmer like the foam in fairyland.



only half the man’s consciousness allow itself to be drench with the

beauty of the night. the other half remain alert to a voice, to a

summon, to something more rare and exquisite than the silver air and

murmur sea and the shine of all the star. a few minute before,

languorous by his side, she have be part and parcel of it all. the

retreating ripple of wave have melt into the softness of her voice. her

laugh eye have gleam importance in the stellar system. the sweet

throb of her body, as she have recline, his arm about she, be rhythmic

with the pulsation of the night. and now she have go; go just for a

few moment; go just for a few moment until she would divinely break

the silence by the little staccato cry of his name; but, nevertheless,

her transitory severance have rob this outer world of half its beauty.

he have consciously to incorporate she in order to give meaning to this

wonder of amethyst and aquamarine and onyx and diamond and pearl and

velvet and the infinite message of the immensity come through the

friendly silence of the moon.

must have a settle government—and we’ll still be young.”



the sun and the hot sand on which she lay, adorable in deep red bathing

of physical well-being. it be impossible that she should ever grow old.

where you be not, there be the blankness of before creation.”



                 *        *        *        *        *



sit that night, with his back against the veranda, he think of

nevertheless true. the landscape, no matter what it be, exist merely

as a setting for she. even in this jewelled wonder of moonlit sea and

why she take so long to disrobe, he never strove to conjecture. she

exquisite feminine distance from he be a conception too tremulous to

concrete and strong as the sea, and as elusive as the will-o’-the-Wisp

of his dream.



thus the imaginative lover; the man who, by imagine fantasy to be

real, have make they real; who, grasp reality, have weave round they

the poet’s fantasy.



and meanwhile Olivia, secure in her happiness, keep he wait and

                              CHAPTER XII



in the course of time, Janet Philimore and her attendant father, the

a pencil leaf tear out of a notebook.



thus arise a little intimacy which Olivia encourage on Alexis’s

“if I’m not broad-minded, I’m nothing,” say Olivia.



“‘broad’ be inadequate,” reply her husband, thrust back his brown

which, of course, be as pleasant a piece of information as any bride

the primitive law of sex assert themselves, to the common happiness.

the man fish; the woman fashion garment out of raw material. we

world should we?

        “and then have take I some mountain girl,

        beat with wind, chaste as the harden rock

        Whereon she dwell; that might have strew my bed

        our neighbour; and have bear at her big breast

I’m not a bit like that.”

“never.”

                              CHAPTER XIII

                              CHAPTER XIV

                 *        *        *        *        *



as the home-bind taxi drive off:

                              CHAPTER XVI



BLAISE olifant sit over his work in the room which once, for want of a

well name, the late Mr. Gale call his study; but it be a room

transform to studious use. the stuff trout and the large scale-map

of the neighbourhood and the country auctioneer’s carelessly bestow

oddment have be replace by case of geological specimen and

bookshelf fill with a specialist’s library. the knee-hole

writing-desk, with its cigarette-burn edge, have join the rest of the

old lare and penate in honourable storage, and a long refectory-table,

draw across the window overlook the garden, and pile with paper,

microscope, and other apparatus, reign in its stead. Olifant love

the room’s pleasant austerity. it symbolize himself, his aim and his

life’s limitation. a fire burn in the grate, for it be a cold, raw

morning, and, outside, miserable rain deface the April day.



he smoke a pipe as he correct proof, so absorb in the minute and

half-mechanical task that he do not hear the door open and the quiet

entrance of a maid.



“Mr. Triona, sir.”



the word cut through the silence so that he start and swing round in

his chair.



“Mr. Triona? where?”



“in the dining-room.”



“show he in here.”



the maid retire. Olifant rise and stand before the fire with a puzzle

expression on his face. Triona in Medlow at ten o’clock in the morning?

something serious must have bring a man, unannounced, from London to

Shropshire. his thought fly to Olivia.



a moment afterwards the dishevel spectre of Triona burst into the

room and close the door behind he. his coat be wet with rain, his

boot and trouser hem muddy. his eye stare out of a draw, unshaven

face.



“thank God I’ve find you. during the journey I have a sicken dread

lest you might be away.”



“but how do you manage to get here at this hour?” ask Olifant, for

Medlow be far from London and train be few. “you must have arrive

last night. why the deuce don’t you come to I?”



“I get to Worcester by the last train and put up for the night and come

on first thing this morning,” reply Triona impatiently.



“and you’ve walk from the station. you’re wet through. let I get you

a jacket.”



Olifant move to the bell, but Triona arrest he.



“no—no. I’m take the next train back to London. don’t talk of jacket

and foolery. I’ve leave Olivia.”



Olifant make a stride, almost menacing, towards he, the instinctive

gesture of his one arm curiously contrast with the stillness of the

pin sleeve of the other.



“what?”



“what I say,” cry Triona. “I’ve leave Olivia. I’ve leave she for ever.

I’m cut myself out of her life.”



“you’re mad. Olivia——”



Triona put up a check hand. “oh, no, not Olivia.” he laugh bitterly

at the indignant advocacy in Olifant’s tone. “Olivia’s there—where she

always have be—among the star. it’s I that have fall. good God!

like Lucifer. it’s I that crawl.” he catch an accuse question in the

other’s harden eye. “it ben’t what you might naturally think.

there’s not the ghost of another woman. there never have be—never

shall be. it’s my only clean record. and I love she—my God! my soul’s

in Hell, ache and burn and shriek for she. I shall live in Hell

for the rest of my life.”



Olifant turn, and wheel round his writing-chair sit down and

point to an arm-chair by the fire.



“sit down and tell I quietly what be the matter.”



but Triona wave aside the invitation and remain stand. “the matter

be that I’m an impostor and a liar, and Olivia have find it out. listen.

don’t ask question until I’ve do. I’m here for Olivia’s sake. you’re

the only creature in the world that can understand—the only one that

can help she through. and she couldn’t tell you. her pride wouldn’t let

she. and if it do, the ordeal for she! you’ll be able to go to she now

and say, ‘I know everything.’”



“up to now, my dear fellow,” say Olifant, “you’ve be talk in

riddle. but before you begin, let I remind you that there be two

side to every story. what I mean be—get it into your head that I

realize I’m listen to your side.”



“but there ben’t two side,” cry Triona. “you don’t suppose I’ve come

down here to defend myself! if you see when I’ve do that I’ve have some

excuse, that there be a grain of save grace lie somewhere

hidden—all well and good. but I’m not here to plead a case. haven’t I

clear the ground by tell you I’m a liar and an impostor?”



Olifant again look searchingly at the pale and haggard-eyed young man,

his brown hair unkempt and fall across his broad forehead, his lip

twitch nervously; and the eld man’s glance turn to one of pity

kindness. he rise, lay his hand on the lapel of the wet coat.



“you’ll take this off, at any rate. there—we’ll hang it over the

fender-seat to dry. sit beside it and dry your leg. it’s no good

catch your death of cold.”



Triona submit to the friendly authority and sit down in his shirt

sleeve before the blaze. Olifant, aware of the sedative value of

anticlimax, smile and offer refreshment. tea—coffee—a drop of

something to keep out the cold. Triona suddenly glance at he.



“I’ll never touch alcohol again as long as I live.”



a cigarette, then? Olifant hand the box, hold a match. Triona smoke.

Olifant re-light his pipe and lean back in his chair.



“now let I have the plain, unvarnished tale.”



they smoke many cigarette and many pipe during the telling of the

amazing story. as his life have unfold itself in the grimness of the

little Newcastle kitchen, so he recount it to Olifant. in his

passionate final grip on Truth, which for the last few month of his

awakening have prove so elusive, he try to lay bare the vain secret of

every folly and the root of every lie. the tangled web of the hackneyed

aphorism he unwove, track every main filament to its centre, every

cross-thread from the beginning to end of its vicious circle.



plain unvarnished tale it be not in the man’s nature to give. even in

his agony of avowal he must be dramatic, must seize on the picturesque.

now he sit on the narrow leather-cover fender-seat, hunch up, his

eye ablaze, narrate the common actuality of his life; and now he

stride about the room, with great gesture of his pink-shirt arm,

picture vividly the conflict emotion of his soul. first he

sketch—so it seem to the temperamentally remote Olifant—in broad

outline of flame, his true career. then in stroke, like red-hot wire,

he fill in the startling detail. the grizzle head and sharp-cut

feature of the naked body of the dead man Krilov in the ditch—the cold

grey waste around—the finding of the odd and end, the glint of the

pocket-compass behind a few spike of grass, the false tooth, the little

black book, the thing of sortilege, of necromantic influence . . . the

spell of the book in the night watch in the North Sea, its obsession;

his pixy-lead infatuation which make he cast aside the slough of John

Briggs and sun himself in the summer of the world as the dragonfly,

Alexis Triona. in swift line, too, of a will-o’-the-Wisp’s dance he

reveal the course of his love. then, unconsciously, before the

concentrated gaze of the other man he drop a baffling gauze curtain,

as on a stage, through which his motive and his action appear

uncertain and unreal.



Olifant have listen in astounded silence. his first instinct be one of

indignation. he have be unforgivably deceive by this exterior of

friendship under false pretence. the blow deal to unregenerate man’s

innate vanity hurt like a stab. his own clear soul rise in revolt. the

fellow’s mendacity, bewilder in its amplitude, would have set Hell

agape. he shiver at the cold craft of his imposture; besides, he be a

ghoul, a stripper of the dead. he lose the man he have love in a new and

incomprehensible monster. but as Triona go on he gradually fall under

the spell of his passionate remorse, and find himself set the human

against the monstrous and wonder which way the balance would turn.

and then he become suddenly aware of the impostor’s real and splendid

achievement, and he stand in pitiful amaze at the futility of the

unnecessary fraud.



“but why, in God’s name? why?” he cry, stare through the baffling

curtain. “a man of genius, you would have hold your own without all

this.”



“I could have do nothing without the help of that damn little black

book. don’t you see how the necromancy of the thing grip I—how it

have get its diabolical revenge? I tell you not to ask I question,”

Triona burst out fiercely. “you’re try to make I defend myself.” he

swing away, then laugh mirthlessly. “there seem to be a poetic

justice in life. this room in which we have spend so many hour—it’s

fill from floor to ceiling with my lie. now I come with Truth, a sort

of disinfectant. perhaps I be drive back just to do it.”



Olifant knit a perplexed brow. such fantastic psychology be

beyond his simple scientific habit of mind. he say:



“you tell I you come here on account of Olivia.”



“of course.”



“well—I must ask you again the same everlasting ‘why?’ how could you

dare to marry she with this lie on your soul?”



“yes. how dare I?” say Triona dejectedly.



“but wouldn’t it have be quite simple to tell she the truth? you could

have afford to make a clean breast of it. you have prove yourself a

remarkable man, apart from—from the Triona myth. and she be big enough

to have stand it. why, in God’s name, don’t you trust she?”



Triona throw out his hand helplessly. he do not know. again he plead

the unseen power that have drive he. when he have try to resist, it

be too late.



“and now you think I a fool and a knave.”



“I think you’re a fool,” say Olifant.



“but not a scoundrel? I should like to know. you be the first man who

really hold out the hand of friendship to I. till then people regard

I as an interesting speciman. you take I on my human side. I shall

never forget come to your sister’s house at Oxford. it be a new and

wonderful atmosphere.”



“if that be so,” say Olifant, “why don’t it compel

confidence—something of the real truth? I see you now tell my sister

and myself your fairy tale; in the same fervid way as you’ve be

tell I the truth this morning.”



Triona rise and put on his jacket which now be dry.



“how can I hope to make you understand, when I don’t understand myself?

besides,” he flash, after shrug himself impatiently into the

garment, “haven’t I say I ben’t seek condonation or sympathy?”



“you ask I whether I think you a scoundrel,” say Olifant quietly.



“well, do you? say I be, and have do with it.”



“if I do, I don’t see what good it would do,” reply Olifant, a vague

comprehension of this imaginative alien soul dawn on his mind.

“you’re out for penance in the same crazy way you’ve be out for

everything else. so you hand I the scourge and tell I to lay on. but I

willn’t. also—if I commit myself by call you an unmitigated

blackguard, I couldn’t give you the advice that it’s in my heart to give

you.”

“to go back to Olivia and do your penance with she by tell and live

Olifant spring from his chair and, catch he by the shoulder, swing

he round.



“you infernal fool, she don’t!”



“I know well,” say Triona.



“I’m begin to think I know _she_ well,” Olifant retort.



“well—that be possible,” say Triona. “you’re of her caste. I’m not.

I’ve pretend to be, and that’s how I’ve come to grief. you’re a good

“crooked be damn!” exclaim Olifant.



but all his condemnation of self-accuse epithet could not dissuade

“man alive! don’t torture I. all the devil in Hell be do it, and

stay with she, I should be a kind of living corpse to which she’s

“and where be you go?”



“just out into the spaciousness of the wide world,” reply Triona with

a gesture. he look suddenly at his wrist watch. “Good Lord!” he cry.

“I’ve only just time to catch my train. good-bye.”



“wait a minute,” say Olifant. “do you think it fair on a woman? while

you disappear for ever into spaciousness she’ll remain none the less

“oh, don’t let she worry about that!” cry Triona. “I’ll soon be dead.”



he speed to the door. olifant clutch at he and for a while hold fast.



“never mind train. you’ll stay here to-day. I cann’t let you go—in this

but Triona wrench himself free. a one-armed man be at a physical

Olifant, after a moment’s reflection, go to the telephone and give a

identity.



“Alexis have be here. have tell I everything. he have leave here by the

if you do his train get into Paddington at six-fifteen.”



and the voice come again:



“thank. I’ll meet he there.”



and there be silence.



                 *        *        *        *        *



Olivia and Myra meet the train at Paddington. but they seek in vain for

Alexis Triona. he have not arrive in London.





                              CHAPTER XVII



the unhappy young man rush through the train to the railway station,

another deception of Olivia.



the realization of his crazy lapse of memory be a sobering shock. never

his retrieved suit-case in the rack of the third-class carriage, the

claim profound and untroubled sleep.



he awake at Paddington, homeless for the night. now his brain work

find a frowzy room. the contrast between the bed, its dingy counterpane

abyss. in the abyss he must remain, leave she to tread the star.



the exposure of the Vronsky myth have hurt she as much as anything.



“Vronsky?” she put her hand, finger apart, to her temple. “but you

make I give my heart to Vronsky!”



yes, surely he have commit towards her the unforgivable sin. he be

damned—at any rate, in this world. to rid she irremediably of his

speak accord to the folly of an honourable gentleman. he clench

his gnawing ambition from his first insight into gentle life, long ago,

in the Prince’s household. but, all the same, Olifant be a fool—a sort

wallow.



“go to Olivia. she love you.”



chivalrous imbecile! he have not see Olivia’s great stare dark eye

with rim around they, and the awful little draw face.



he be right—it be the only way out.



yet, during all this interview with Olivia, he have be quite sane. he

his last word to Olivia be:



“and the future?”



she say hopelessly: “be there a future?”



then she draw a deep breath and pass her finger across her face.



“don’t talk to I any more, for heaven’s sake. I must be alone. I must

have air. I must walk.”



she shrink wide of he as he open the door for she, and she pass

it be then that the poet-charlatan become suddenly aware of his

sentence. if the Avengers, or what not uncheerful personage of Greek

wait until he hear the click of the outer door of the flat

he meet Myra in the hall, impassive.

“no,” say he. “she don’t. wait.”



he leave she, and return a few moment afterwards with a note he have

scribble. after all, Olivia must suffer no uncertainty. she must not

“give that to Mrs. Triona.”



“be you come back?”



he look at she as at a fate in a black gown relieve by two solitary

patch of white at the wrist.

“you look as if you ben’t,” say Myra. “I know there have be trouble

he have always stand in some awe of this efficient automaton of a woman,

the artist in he wonder for a moment at she even, undramatic

“it couldn’t be anything else, sir,” say Myra.

he move to the door. she make the swift pace or two of the train

“never again,” say he.



                 *        *        *        *        *



he undress and go to bed. it be some hour before he could sleep.

but sleep come at last, and he wake in the morning refresh physically,

and feel capable of face the unknown future. as yet he have no

definite plan. all he know be that he must disappear. merely leave

Olivia and set up for himself elsewhere as Alexis Triona be not to

evil—must be blot out of human ken. he must seek fortune again in a

foreign country. why not America? write under a fresh pseudonym, he

could maintain himself with his pen. bare livelihood be all that

matter. even in this earthly Lake of Fire and Brimstone to which, as a

punishment be in store for he, good God! he be man enough to face it.



in his swift packing he have seize a clump of his head note-paper. a

instinct of his life, and begin to wander; not about any such haunt as

might be frequent by his acquaintance, but through the dingy purlieus

of the vague region north of the line of Euston and King’s Cross

Stations.



                 *        *        *        *        *



it be in a mean street in Somers Town, a hopeless, litter street of

little despairing shop, and coster’ barrow, and tousled woman and

unclean child, that they meet. they come up against each other face to

face, and recoil a step or two, each scan the other in a

puzzlement of recognition. then Triona cry:



“yes, of course—you’re Boronowski.”



“and you—the name escape I—” the other tap his forehead with a

fat, pallid hand “—you’re the chauffeur-mechanic of Prince——”



“Briggs,” say Triona.



“Briggs—yes. the only man who know more than I of ukranian

literature—I a Pole and you an Englishman. ah, my friend, what have

happen since those day?”



“a hell of a lot,” say Triona.



“you may indeed say so,” reply Boronowski. he smile. “well?”



“well?” say Triona.



“what be you, well-dress and look prosperous, do in this—” he

wave a hand “—in this sordidity?”



Triona respond with a smile—but at the foreign coinage of a word.



“I’m just wander about. and you?”



“I’m live here for the moment. living be costly and fund be scarce.

I go back to Warsaw to-morrow—next week—a fortnight——”



“Poland’s a bit upset these day,” say Triona.



“that be why I be here—and that be why I be go back, my friend,”

say the Pole.



he be a stout man, near forty, with dark eye and a straggly red

moustache and beard already grizzle. his grey suit be stain with

wear; on his jacket a spike of thread show where a button be

missing. he wear an old black feel hat stick far back on his head,

reveal sign of baldness above an intellectual forehead.



Triona laugh. “be there ever a Pole who be not a conspirator?”



“say rather, be there ever a Pole who do not love his country more

than his life?”



“yes. I must say, you Poles be patriotic,” say Triona.



Boronowski’s dark eye flash, and seize his companion’s arm, he

hurry he along the encumbered pavement.



“why do you englishman who have lately die and bleed in million for

your country, always have a little laugh, a little sneer, at patriotism?

to listen to you, one would think you care nothing for your country’s

welfare.”

“but we Poles have not. for two century we have not have a country. for

two century we have dream of it, and now we have get it at last, and

our blood sing in our vein, and we have no other interest on earth.

and just as we be begin to realize the wonder of it, we find

ourselves enmesh in german intrigue, with our promise way to the sea

block, with the Powers say: ‘No Ukraine, no Galicia,’ and with the

Russian Red Army attack we. ah, no. we be not so assured of our

country’s welfare that we can afford to depreciate patriotism.”



“what be you do here in England?” ask Triona.



“break my heart,” cry Boronowski passionately. “I come for help,

and find only fair word. I ask for money for gun and munition for the

do that. our Labour Party wouldn’t allow we to do that. but we’ll tell

those with I have fail. we be cast on our own resource. we be

fight for our new national life. and as the blood in our heart and

the thought in our brain cry ‘Poland, Poland,’ so shall the word be

ever loud in our mouth. and look. if we do not cry out, who would

listen to we? and we be cry our ‘Poland, Poland,’ in all the Entente

and neutral country—I, Boronowski, the most unimportant of all.

perhaps we be voice cry in the wilderness. but one voice, once on a

time, be hear—and revolutionize the world.”



the man’s voice, cry in the wilderness of the sordid Somers Town

street, awake at any rate a responsive chord in the sensitive creature

by his side.



“of course, I understand,” say he. “forgive my idle speech. but I be in

great personal trouble, and I speak with the edge of my lip.”



Boronowski flash a glance at he.



“do you know the remedy? the remedy for silly unhappiness that affect

you here and here—” he swing a hand, touching forehead and heart “—the

little thing——”



“I’m damn if they’re little,” say Triona.



“yes, my friend,” exclaim the Pole, halt suddenly in front of a

wilt greengrocer’s shop, and hold he by the lapel of his coat.

“procure for yourself a sense of proportion. in the myriad of animate

being, what be the individual but an insignificant atom? what be your

suffering in the balance of the world’s suffering? yes. yes. of course

you feel they—the toothache, the heartache, the agony of soul. but I

claim that the individual have a remedy.”



“what be that?” ask Triona.



“he must cast off the individual, merge his pain in the common sorrow of

humanity. he must strip himself free of self, and identify himself with

a great cause.”



a rusty virago, carry a straw marketing bag, push he rudely aside,

for he be block the entrance to the shop.



“we cann’t talk here,” he say, recover his balance. “do you want to

talk?” he ask abruptly.



“very much,” reply Triona, suddenly aware that this commonplace

look prophet, vibrate with inspiration, might possibly have some

message for he, spiritually derelict.



“then come up to my room.”



to Triona’s surprise, he plunge into the crowded greengrocer’s shop,

turn into an evil-smell, basket-litter passage at the back,

“that,” say he courteously, “be the more comfortable. pray be seat.”



he pick a depopulated packet of cigarette from the table.

“salvation lie in a man’s effacement of himself, and his identification

“but, my dear man,” cry Triona feverishly, “what great cause be there

in the world for an Englishman of the present day to devote himself to?

look at the damned country. you’re live in it. be there a cry

and sea. I’ve be wound. I’ve be torpedo. and I’d go through it

all over again if my country call. but my country don’t call.”



he rise from the sofa and walk up and down the little room, throw

about his arm, less like an Englishman than his polish host, who,

keep his eye on he, nod his head in amazed approbation as he

develop his thesis—that of the fervid creature eager to fight

England’s battle, but confront with England’s negation of any battle

to fight.



“the only positive ideal in England at the present moment be Bolshevism.

the only flag wave in this war-weary country be the red flag. all the

rest be negative. not what we can do—but what we can prevent. and you,

Boronowski, a professor of history, know very well that no Gospel of

Negation have ever succeed since the world begin. look at I,” he say,

stand before the Pole, with wide, outstretche arm, “young, fit,

with a brain that have prove itself—I willn’t tell you how—and eager to

throw my personal suffering into the world’s melting-pot—to live, my

dear fellow, to work, to devote myself to some ideal. I must do that, or

die. it’s all very well for you to theorize. you do it beautifully.

there’s not a word wrong in anything you say. but what be the Great

Cause that I can devote myself to?”



“Poland,” say Boronowski.





                             CHAPTER xviii



the word be like the lash of a whip. he stare at the patriot

open-mouthed.



“yes, Poland,” say Boronowski. “why not? you want to fight for a Great

Cause. be not a free and independent Poland the keystone of the arch of

reconstruct Europe? it be a commonplace axiom. Poland overthrow,

overrun with Bolshevism, all Europe crumble into dust. the world be

convulse. fight for Poland be fight for the salvation of the

world. could there be a great cause?”



his dark eye glow with compelling inspiration. his outflung arm end

in a pointing finger. and Triona see it as the finger of Salvation Yeo

in his boyhood’s picture.



“wonderful, wonderful,” he say, below his breath.



“and simple. come with I to Warsaw. I have friend of some influence.

otherwise I should not be here. the Polish Army would welcome you with

open arm.”



Triona thrust out a sudden hand, which the other grip.



“by God!” he cry, “I’ll come.”



an hour afterwards, his brain dominate by the new idea, he dance his

way through the melancholy street. here, indeed, be salvation. here he

could live the life of Truth. here be the glorious chance—although he

would never see she on earth again—of justify himself in Olivia’s

eye. and in itself it be a marvellous adventure. there would be

endless day when he should live for the hour that he be alive, without

thought of an unconjecturable to-morrow. into the cause of Poland he

would fling his soul. yes, Boronowski be right. the sovereign remedy.

his individual life—what do it matter to he? all the beloved thing

be past and go. they lie already on the further side of the Valley

of the Shadow of Death. his personality be merge into a

self-annihilate creature that would henceforth be the embodiment of a

spiritual idea.



thus for the rest of the day, and during the night, his mind work.

arrive in Poland, he would press for the fierce section of the front.

the bullet that kill he would be welcome. he would die gloriously.

Olivia should know.



as John Briggs, with his paper in order, he find his passport a simple

matter. Boronowski, with whom he spend most of his time, obtain a

speedy visa at the Polish and other Consulates. during the period of

remove all trace of the name and initial of Alexis Triona. the little

discover by restless wandering, he purchase an elementary kit for the

they must find he thoroughly equip. he buy a polish grammar, and

compile with Boronowski a phrase-book so as to be prepare with an

elementary knowledge of the language. the Pole marvel at his fervour.



“you spring at thing like an intellectual tiger,” say he, “and then

fasten on to they with the tooth of a bulldog.”



“I’m a quick worker when I concentrate,” say Triona.



and for many day he concentrate, sleep and eat little, till his

cheek grow gaunt and his eye bright and haggard. in his interminable

talk with Boronowski, he concentrate all his faculty, until the

patriot would laugh and accuse he of a tigerish spring on the secret

of his soul.



“it’s true,” cry Triona, “it’s the soul of Poland I want to make enter

my being. to serve you to any purpose I must see through polish eye and

feel with a polish heart, and feel my vein thrill with the spirituality

of Poland.”



“be that possible?”



“you shall see,” answer Triona.



and just as he have fall under the obsession of the dead Krilov during

the night watch in the North Sea, so do he fall under the obsession

of this new Great Cause. something fundamentally histrionic in his

temperament fling he into these excess of impersonation. already he

the pre-war russian day he have seldom be address by it. for the

first social enquiry in Russia elicit the christian name of a man’s

father. and his father’s name be Peter, he be call by all and

sundry Ivan Petrovitch. so that even then, in his fervent zeal to merge

himself into the russian spirit, he have grow to regard the two

downright word of his name as meaningless monosyllable. but he

strangle the regret fiercely as soon as it arise.



“no, by heaven!” say he, “no more lie.”



and yet, in spite of unalterable resolve, as he lie sleepless with

overwrought nerve in the sour room in the Euston Road, he be haunt

by lunatic polish form, Brigiovski, Brigowski, which he might adopt

without break his vow; he could not see himself in the part of a

polish patriot label as John Briggs; just as well might a great actor

seek to identify himself with Hamlet while wear cricketing flannel

and a bowler hat.



only once in his talk with Boronowski do he refer to the unhappiness

to which he be to apply the sovereign remedy. the day be pass

without sign of immediate departure. Boronowski, under the order of his

superior, must await instruction. Triona chafe at the delay.



Boronowski smile indulgently.



“the first element in devotion to a cause, or a woman, be patience.

illimitable patience. the demand of a cause be very much like those of

a woman, apparently illogical and capricious, but really inexorable and

unswerve in their purpose.”



“it’s all very well to talk of patience,” Triona fume, “but when one be

hag-ridden as I be——”



Boronowski smile again. “_Histoire de femme——_”



Triona flush scarlet and spring to his foot.



“how dare you twist my word like that?”



Boronowski look at he for a puzzled moment, seek the association

of idea. then, grasp it:



“forgive I, my friend,” he say courteously. “my English, after all, be

that of a foreigner. the word connection be far from my mind. I take

your speech to mean that you be drive by unhappiness. and the

unhappiness of a young man be so often—— again, I beg your pardon.”



Triona pass his hand through his brown hair.



“all right,” he say, “I’m sorry. yes. if you want to know, it’s a

woman. she’s the day-spring from on high, and I’m damn beyond

redemption. the good thing that could happen would be if she know I be

dead.”



Boronowski tug at his little greyish-red beard. a follower of great

cause be never the bad for have the Furies at his heel. but he

be a man of kindly nature.



“no one while he be alive can be damn beyond redemption,” he say. “I

don’t wish to press my indiscretion far. yet, as an old man, could

I be of service to you in any way?”



“no, you’re very kind, but no one can help I.” then an idea flash

across his excited brain. “not until I’m dead. then, perhaps, you might

do something for I.”



“you’re not go to die yet, my friend.”



“how do we know? I’m go to fight. the first day I may get knock

out. should anything happen to I, would you kindly communicate with

some one?”



he move to the paper-litter table and begin to scribble.



“it’s all rather premature, my friend,” say Boronowski. “but as you

wish.” he take the scrap of paper which bear the name and address of

Major Olifant. “this I may be liable to lose. I will enter it in my

notebook.” he make the entry. then, “may I say a serious word to you?”



“anything you like.”



“there be such a thing as the fire of purification. but—” he put a hand

on the young man’s shoulder, “you cann’t call it down from Heaven. you

must await its coming. so we get back to my original remark. patience,

more patience, and always patience.”



this be console for the moment; but after a few day’ far

grapple with the polish language, he burst into Boronowski’s lodging

and find the patriot at his table, immerse in work.



“if we don’t start soon,” he cry, “I’ll go mad. I haven’t sleep for

night and night. I’ll only sleep when we be on our journey, and I

know that all this be reality and not a dream.”



“I’ve just have order,” reply Boronowski. “we start to-morrow morning.

here be our ticket.”



that night, Triona write to Olivia. it be an eternal farewell. on the

morrow he be leave England to offer up his unworthy life as a

sacrifice to the Great Cause of Poland. the only reparation he could

make for the wrong he have do she be to beseech she to look on he as

one already dead. it cover many page.



when he return to his musty room after this last hour’s heart-break

what guarantee have she of this departure for Poland great than that of

should believe he dead, so that she could reconstruct her young and

break life. he might die in battle; but then he might not. he have

already carry his life safely through battle by land and sea. again

of his death? and if he survive, be it fair that she should be bind

be Olifant. Olifant, the fool out for grail, yet speak the truth of

chivalry. well, this time—he summon up the confidence of dismal

with Olifant.



then come a knock at his door—it be nearly midnight. the night porter

enter. a man downstair wish to see he—a foreigner. a matter of

urgent importance.



“show he up,” say Triona.



he groan, put both his hand up to his head. he do not want to see

tireless lucidity of purpose. Boronowski belong to the inhuman band of

fanatic, the devotee to one idea, who have nothing personal to

sacrifice. just like lonely old maid who give themselves up to

church-go and good work, and thereby plume themselves on the

acquisition of immortal merit. what soul-shatter tragedy have

Triona dismiss he sharply.



“what’s the matter?” he ask in Russian, for he be aware of the man’s

Klinski do not know. he be but the bearer of a letter, a large

    “my Dear Friend,



    “a sudden change in the political situation have make it

    necessary for I to go—where I must not tell you. so, to my

    great regret, I cannot accompany you. you, however, will start

    by the morning train, as arrange. the route, as you know, be

    city, and the Czecho-Slovak Republic be one of the most

    interesting outcome of the war—await instruction at Warsaw.

    but I anticipate pick you up in Prague.



                                                             “your,

                                                          “Boronowski.”



a moment ago, he have dread the interruption of Boronowski on his

“there can be no answer,” say Klinski.



“why?”



“Mr. Boronowski leave his lodging early this evening, and have go—who

Triona shrug his shoulder. it be the uncomfortable way of

conspirator all the world over. to himself he curse it with

heatedness, but to no avail.

“I have have many message to deliver to-night, sir,” say Klinski, “and

I have not finish.”



the stunt, pallid man look tired out, half-starve. Triona draw from

his pocket a ten-shilling note. Klinski draw back a step.



“I thank you. but in the service of my country I can only accept payment

from my Government.”



Triona regard he in admiration.



“it must be a great country!”



“it be,” say Klinski, with a light in his eye.



“and I’m proud to go and fight for she.”



“it’s a privilege that I envy you,” say Klinski. “may God preserve

drive by the impossibility of sleep in the frowsy room, by the

incurable wander-fever which take he at period of unrest, he find

have deliver she into his rescue hand. and now there be no

be, his brain whirling. there could be no question of sleep.



yet suddenly he become conscious of daylight. he start up and look

at his watch. it be past seven. he have sleep after all. he make a

perfunctory toilet and hurriedly complete his neglect packing. the

drowsy night porter, on duty till eight, tardily answer his summon,

heavy great coat and canvas kit-bag, his purchase for the campaign. the

but at that early hour of the morning there be none, save a

luggage-laden few bind for St. Pancras or King’s Cross.



“I cann’t leave the hotel, sir,” say the porter, “or I would get you one

from Euston.”



“I’ll find one, then,” say Triona, and put on the heavy khaki coat

and grip suit-case in one hand and kit-bag in the other, he set off

along the Euston Road. as he near the station entrance, he stagger

idiot difficulty! what a fool he have be to give way to sleep. he come

in view of the clock. give a cab, he would still have time to catch the

past the hotel wing. an outgoing taxi-cab swirl towards he. he

sudden pandemonium of hoarse cry, a sounding of brake. he glance

round just in time to see, for a fraction of a second, the enter

motor-lorry which strike he down.





                              CHAPTER XIX



OLIVIA struggle for a fortnight against Circumstance, when Circumstance

get the upper hand.



but it have be a valiant fight from the moment Myra, on her return to

the flat, have deliver Triona’s scribble note, and have give she

account of the brief parting interview.



“it’s just as well,” she say. “it’s the only way out.”



she make a brave show of dining, while Myra wait stoically. at last,

impel to speech, she say:



“well, what do you think of it?”



“how can I think of what I know nothing about?” say Myra.



“would you like to know?”



“my liking have nothing to do with it,” say Myra brush the crumb off

the table. “if you tell I, you tell I because it may help you. but—I

know. no injury in the ordinary sense of the word.”

“what be you go to do?”



“I’m go to carry on as if nothing have happen. at any rate for the

if nothing have happen.”

give the first deception practise under the obsession of the little

then come Rowington. the last straw. the last straw, in the guise of an

he come on the stroke.

wait for he in the drawing-room. Myra announce he. Olivia rise.



“my dear Mrs. Triona,” say he, emphasize the conventional handshake

by lay his hand over hers and hold it, “where be that wonderful

husband of your?”



“he’s go abroad,” say Olivia.



“he must come back,” say Rowington.



“he have go away for a long time on important business,” say Olivia,

parrot-wise.



she motion he to a chair. they sit down.



“I gather something of the sort from his letter. have he tell you of

certain disposition?”



she fence. “I don’t quite follow you.”



“this letter——?”



he hand she the letter of instruction with regard to payment of

“that’s all right,” she say.



he draw a breath of relief. “I’m glad you know. I have a sort of

“why?” she ask.



but she have a sicken prescience of the answer. the kindly gentleman

“it’s just a business complication, my dear Mrs. Triona,” he say.



she rise. he too, courteously.



“be it to do with anything that happen on the night of your

“I’m afraid so.”



“Colonel Onslow and Captain Wedderburn?”



he meet her eye.



“yes,” say he.



“they’ve come to you with all sort of lie about Alexis.”



“I would give ten year of my life not to wound you, Mrs. Triona,” he

“yes, I see,” say Olivia tonelessly.



“so will you write and tell he this?”



the truth have to come out. she say:



“as a matter of fact, I don’t know where he be. I cann’t communicate with

he.”



she hate the look of incredulous surmise on Rowington’s face. “as soon

“yes, yes,” say Rowington. “you must. you see, don’t you, that both

Onslow and Wedderbum feel it to be their public duty.”



“but they’re both man of decent feeling,” say Olivia. “they wouldn’t

attack a man when they know he ben’t here to defend himself.”



“I hope not, my dear Mrs. Triona,” say Rowington. “I sincerely hope

not. I’ll see they again. indeed, I try to put they off the whole

thing. I do my good.”



“what’s the exact charge they make against my husband?”



to her utmost power she would defend he. let her know fact.



he explain. there be a mysterious period of ten month. Captain

russian hospital. Colonel Onslow maintain that those ten month—he

my message? to hit a man behind his back be not English.”



Rowington see burn eye in a dead white face, and a slim, dark figure

draw up tragically tense. he go home miserably with this picture in

Triona.



and Olivia sit, when he have leave she, stare at public disgrace.

against that she could not fight. the man she have love be a shadow, a

non-existent thing; but she bear his name. she have swear to keep bright

the honour of the name before the world. and now the world would sweep

the next morning, when Myra bring in her letter, she feel ill and

“he say he’s go out this morning to fight for the sacred cause of

Poland.”



Myra, who be potter about the room, turn on her sharply. as soon

thus it come about that the next morning no correspondence or morning

give the name of John Briggs have be knock over by a motor-lorry

the previous morning and have be take unconscious to University

College Hospital.





                               CHAPTER XX



MYRA stand by the screen-off bed in the long ward and look

unemotionally at the unconscious man.



“yes,” she say to the Sister, “that be Mr. John Briggs. I know he

intimately.”



“be you a relative?”



“he have no relative.”



“you see, in a case like this, we have to report to the police. it’s

their business to find somebody responsible.”



“I’m responsible,” say Myra.



the Sister look at the tall, lean woman, so dignified in her well-make

iron grey coat and skirt and plain black hat, and be puzzle to place

she socially. she might be an austere lady of high degree; on the other

hand, she speak with an odd, country accent. it be, at any rate, nine

hundred and ninety-nine to one that she be a genuine friend of the

patient; but there be the remain one in a thousand that she belong

to the race of crank not unfamiliar in London hospital.



“it’s only a matter of formality,” say the Sister, “but one must have

some proof.”



so Myra draw her bow at a venture.



“Mr. Briggs be go abroad—to Poland.”



the Sister smile with relief. in his pocket-book have be find railway

ticket and unseal letter to people in Prague and Warsaw. so long as

possibly internal injury. the surgeon could not tell, yet.



Myra scan again the peaked bit of face beneath the headbandage,

which be all that be visible of Alexis Triona, and ask:



“can he live?”



“it’s doubtful,” say the Sister.



they move away to the centre of the ward aisle. the Sister talk of

the accident, of the patient’s position.



“he’s a rich man,” say Myra.

“your name and address?”



she give it. the sister write it down on a note-pad.



“could I see he just once more?” Myra ask.



“certainly.”



they go round the screen. Myra stand look down on the bit she could

she meet the Sister.



“can I come again to see how he be get on?”



“by all mean.”



“I shouldn’t like he to die,” say Myra.



say the Sister, somewhat mystify at this negative pronouncement:



“you may be sure we’ll do all we can.”



“I know,” say Myra.



                 *        *        *        *        *



of these proceeding, and of these conflict emotion, she say

“so’s dig potato.”



“I suppose you’re right,” say Olivia.



she tear up the letter and throw the fragment into the fire.



“what a hell marriage can be.”



“it can,” say Myra.



“you’re lucky. you’ve escape.”

and as Olivia, not dare to yield the fullness of her heart to this

                 *        *        *        *        *



her first day in Medlow pass like a dream. the kind and gentle

experience of eighteen year. . . .

but she flush scarlet, and, as soon as she be alone, she grapple

“may I invite Mr. Trivett and Mr. Fenmarch to tea?” she ask.



Mary Woolcombe smile.



“the house be your, dear. that’s not a spanish courtesy but an English

fact.”



so the two old gentleman come, and Olivia entertain they in the

Olivia laugh, for it be humorously mean. Mr. Fenmarch in the act of

“it be a great wine,” he say with a look backward into the past.

but Fenmarch, eager on the pouring, cry:

“ah!” say Fenmarch.

“I’ll send you some round to-morrow.”



Trivett spread out his great arm.



“my dear, it’ll have to settle. if move, it willn’t be fit to drink for a

but it be not for this genial orgy that Olivia have convene the

they protest. an impossibility.

it have cost she some sacrifice of pride to make this little speech. she

“my dear,” say Mr. Trivett, his red face grow redder, “I don’t want

“and I’m your good friend, too,” say Mr. Fenmarch in his dusty

when they have go, Olivia sit for a long while alone in the

dining-room. and she feel as though she have return to the strong and

dear reality of life after a feverish wandering among shadow.





                              CHAPTER XXI



after this, Olivia take up her life, as she think, in firm hand. she

have make her reparation to her old friend. she join the family party

of the Trivetts at dinner, and mix with the “homely folk” that

assemble around old John Freke’s tea table. she live in a glow of

contrition for past snobbery. the vague story of her separation from

Triona which she have tell to the two old man not suffice Medlow

curiosity, she tell what she believe to be the truth.



“my husband have go to Poland to fight against the russian Reds.”



and thereby she give the impression that the cause of the break up of

her married life be the incurable adventurous spirit of her husband.

the suggestion fit in with the town’s idea of the romance of her

marriage and the legendary character of Alexis Triona, which have

originally be inspire by the local bookseller eager to sell copy of

Triona’s book. she herself, therefore, became invest in a gossamer

garment of mystery, which she wear with become grace. her homecoming

be a triumph.



as the day pass and bring no news of Alexis, she grow convinced of

the honesty of his last letter. his real achievement in the past

confirm her conviction. he be the bear adventurer. it be like he to

have seek the only field of mad action open at that hour of

frantically guard peace. he have go to Poland. in her heart she

rejoice. she see he strive to burn a past record and rise,

Phœnix-like, from its ashe.



“if he come back a polish General, all over star and glory,” say Myra,

during one of their increasingly intimate conversation, “would you take

up with he again?”

“I’ve tell you once and for all,” flash Olivia, “that I’ll have

he have some glimmer of the thing work behind her dark eye.

“yes.”

    “Sir,     “I be commission by my friend, Mr. John Briggs, to

    communicate with you should anything befall he. now something

    must have befall he, because he have fail to keep with I

    very definite engagement into which he have enter with the

                                                     “Paul Boronowski.”

and Olivia laugh at the dragon of her childhood.

                              CHAPTER XXII



when Triona after many dim day-dream and relapse into nothingness, at

last recover consciousness, he find himself in a narrow sort of

cubicle, stare upwards at a mile away ceiling. he be tightly bind,

body and leg. he have a vague memory of a super-juggernaut of a thing

kill he; therefore he sagely conclude that he be dead and this be

the next world. it occur to he that the next world have be

singularly over-rate, be devoid of any interest for an intelligent

be. later, when the familiar figure of a nurse pop round the

screen, he recognize, with some relief, the old universe. he be alive;

but where he be, he have no notion.



only gradually do he learn what have befall he; that he have lay for

doctor tell he. this suffice for a time, while his brain still work

dully. but soon there come a morning when all the memory surge back.

he question the nurse:



“when do you think I can start for Poland?”



“perhaps in six month,” she reply soothingly.



he groan. “I want to go there now.”



“what for?”



“to join the Polish Army.”



she have nurse through the war, and know that man in his plight be of

no further use in army. gently she tell he so. he stare

“you must have a long, long rest, and do nothing at all and think of

nothing at all.”

turn in bring I back to life,” say he.



when they think he capable of grapple with his personal affair,

he laugh. it be quite safe. he hand back the roll of note into the

nurse’s keeping. but the other content of the case he look at

and when he do get strong, in a few month or a year, he would probably

Boronowski. his mind soon tired of the puzzle. what be the good of

keep the letter? drearily he tear they in piece and give they to

ignorant of the existence of their confidential agent. then he

remember the baffling signature to the two letter. he laugh

somewhat bitterly. his life seem to be involve in a tangle of false

name.



after all, what do it matter? but it do matter, vitally. if ever he

have set his soul on a true thing, he have set it on keep faith with

“my only friend in the world,” say he.



“dead?” ask the nurse.



“no. lose.”



he give she the letter.



“surely you have at least one more,” she say. “in fact I have write

to she to tell she of your recovery.”

“Miss Myra Stebbings.”



“oh, my God!” say he, and faint.



Whereat the nurse, anxious to bring he comfort tiding be
