“you haven’t have a long holiday, Myra,” Olivia remark when she

arrive.



“I don’t say I be go on a holiday.”



“I hope thing be all right.”



“as right as they ever can be,” reply Myra.



the weary week of convalescence drag themselves out. Myra do not

come again; and of course he have no other visitor. he make casual

acquaintance in the ward; here and there an ex-soldier with whom he

could exchange reminiscence of warfare.



once a discharged sailor in the next bed—the screen have long since be

remove—recover from an operation, speak to he of mine-sweep

day, and peril of storm and submarine and he grow to regard he as a

brother. both regret the deluging water of the North Sea. the sailor

in these time of peace drive a dust cart for the St. Pancras Borough

Council. the wage be good—but what a life for a sea-fare man! he

would have stick to his old job be it not that a wave have wash he

down on the slithery deck and have bring his knee-cap up against a

stanchion and have stiffen it out so that his career on board-ship be

over. but those be good time, ben’t they? oh yes. of course they

grouse. but they only grouse when they have time. mostly they haven’t.

dust-collecting be an open-air life, true enough; but there be a

difference between the smell of brine and the stench of house refuse. it

be in summer that it make he sick. the odour of the fo’c’sle be not

those of a hairdresser’s shop—nothing smell so fine, he declare, as a

hairdresser’s shop—they be a bit thick, but a man could go on deck

and fill his lung with good salt air. and the grub! what an appetite!

he conjure up gargantuan meal in perilous tempest. nothing of the

sort now. everything he eat taste of sour potato peeling.



“that’s the taste of everything in these post-war day,” say Triona,

“everything in life—sour potato peeling.”



the dustman reckon he be right. in those old day of mine-sweep, a

man have no anxiety. he have no responsibility. he be happy as the

day be long. now he be marry and already have a couple of kid. life

be just one wearisome worry, a continuous accumulation on the debit

side of the slate, with few advantage on the credit side to balance. if

it ben’t the wife it be the boy; if it ben’t the boy, it be the

baby; and if it ben’t they, it be his appendix which have just be

remove. whoever hear of a sailor-man aboard ship get appendicitis?

no, all they thing, say he, be blessing of peace. besides, how be

he go to feed his family when they grow old? and clothe, boot,

school? and he himself—limit to beer—and such beer! he haven’t

taste a drop of rum——. be there anything like it? sometimes he see

it and smell it in his dream, but he always wake up before he could put

his lip to the pannikin. if only one could get something to hold on to

in dream. he never have need to dream of rum in the navy. so much for

peace. give he the good old war again.



and when his wife, a thin lipped, scraggy blonde, with a moth-eat fur

steal round her neck (although it be swelter summer), and a pallid

baby in her arm come to visit he, and speak querulously of domestic

affair, Triona give he his unreserved sympathy.



“and it ain’t,” say the ex-mariner, “as if I couldn’t carry on straight

and proper in civil life. I wonder how many of my mate be get what

I’m get. she ought to be proud of I, she ought. instead of

that—you hear what she say?”



Triona have hear. she have upbraid he for his ungenteel occupation,

consider herself, the daughter (so Triona learn) of a small

sweet-stuff monger in Dover, where they have meet during his sea-go

day, socially degrade by her marriage with a municipal collector of

dust. she have marry he, by the by, before his present appointment,

while he be draw out-of-work pay. apparently he be possess of

some low-comedy histrionic talent, and she be convince that he could

make his fortune as a cinema star.



“you marry?” he ask.



“not now,” say Triona.



“you’ve be through it,” say the misogynist. “woman! there never be a

woman who know when she be well off! oh, Gawd! give I the old day on

the _Barracouta_, where there ben’t any thought of woman. that be my

last ship. I have nine month in she. there be _barracouta_, _Annie

Sandys_, _seahorse_. . . .”



he run through the name of his squadron, forgetful, in the sudden flush

of reminiscence, of domestic care.



“and what do you say you be in?”



“_Vestris._”



“of course. I remember. torpedoed. but even that be well than this?”



Triona agree, and the eternal talk of the sea go on, until the

nostalgia for the wide, free space of the world grip his vital with

the pain of hunger.



“what be you go to do when you come out?” ask the dustman.



“about the same as you,” reply Triona. “what’s the good of a man with

a game leg?”



the dustman sigh. “you’ve get education,” say he.



at first, aware of accent and manner of expression, the dustman have

take he for an ex-officer. only the discharge-paper of John Briggs,

able-seaman, convince he of John Briggs lowly estate. still, in the

_barracouta_ they have an elderly stoker who have be at Cambridge

College. such a man might be his neighbour.



“I run away to sea when I be a boy,” say Triona.



so have the dustman. he wax more confidential. his name be Josh

bunning, and he have sail in every conceivable kind of craft from

Alaska to Singapore. but he have find no time for education. how do his

neighbour acquire it? book? he shake his head. he have be cure of

book on his first voyage, when the second mate catch he read a

tattere manual on gardening, when he ought to have be wash up in

the galley, have kick and cuff he round the deck. Triona’s mind go

back to his boyhood—to an almost identical incident. there be much in

common between himself and Josh Bunnings. they have start on even

term. they have meet on even term in the foul fo’c’sles on the North

Sea. they be on even term, now, lie side by side, lamed, their life

of free adventure a thing of the past. each dread the future; Josh

bunning condemn to cart refuse beneath the affected nose of a shrew

of a wife for the remainder of his day; he, Triona, to deal with such

refuse as the world would leave he, but away from the wife who abhor

he and all his work. on the other hand, between he and Josh Bunnings

lie a great gulf. he have make himself a man of wide culture. Josh

bunning have remain abysmally ignorant. but Josh Bunnings have live

his life an honourable man. if he tell his story to Josh Bunnings he

would be condemn by he, even as he have be condemn by his sister

on the morning of his mother’s funeral. so, when the dustman, with

another sigh, hark back to his former idea and say:



“if only I have education.”



“you’re a damned sight well man than I be, without it,” Triona reply

bitterly.



when the three week’ comradeship come to an end, on the discharge of

Josh Bunnings, he find himself lose again in a friendless world. the

neighbour familiar bed be occupy by an ancient man in the throe

of some ghastly malady, and around he be stretch the horrible,

death-suggest screen. and behind the screen, a week later, the old

man die. it be to relieve the nervous tension of this week that he

begin a correspondence with Josh Bunnings. the write man’s instinct

awake—the mania of self-expression. his letter to the dustman, full of

the atmosphere of the ward, vivid with lightning sketch of

house-surgeon, sister, nurse and patient, with here and there

excursion into contrast tempest, storm of battle, and everywhere

touch with the magic of his queer genius, would, if send to his

literary agent, have gain he a year’s subsistence.



Josh Bunnings visit he occasionally, when free from municipal, and

escape from domestic, obligation. the visit, he explain, be in

return for the letter; for be no scholar, he could not reply. then

one day he appear and sit on the chair by Triona’s bed, with the air

of a man about to bring glad tiding. he be rather a heavy, pallid,

clean-shaven man, with a curl of black hair sweep down to his

eyebrow. his small dark eye gleam. at once he disemburdene his

honest soul. he be a Church of England man; always hold with

church-go—so do his wife; it be the great bond of union between

they. so he be on friendly term with the curate of St. Simon’s. and

be on friendly term with the curate, he have shew he the letter.



“and, would you believe it, mate?” say he. “would you believe it? he

want to put they in print in the Parish Magazine. in print! fancy!”



he slap his thigh. Triona stare at he for a moment and then laugh

out loud for the first time for many week.



“what be you laugh at?” ask the astonished bunning.



“it seem so funny,” say Triona.



“that’s what I think.”



“and a great honour,” say Triona recover.



“of course. only he say he couldn’t print ’em without your permission.”



Triona give permission, stipulate, however, that his name should not

be use. his modesty forbid it he explain. Josh Bunnings go away

delighted. in the course of a few post come a grateful letter from the

curate. in Mr. Briggs’s writing he see sign of considerable literary

talent which he hope Mr. Briggs would cultivate. if he could be of help

in this way, he put his service at Mr. Briggs’s disposal. Triona again

laugh, with grim amusement, at a funny, ironical world.



then, suddenly, the underlie tragedy of this comic interlude smite he

breathless. Alexis Triona be dead and so be his writing, for

evermore. but the impulse to write stir within he so vehemently that

even in these idle letter to Josh Bunnings he have put all his vividness

of literary expression. the curate’s dim recognition of the unusual be

a sign and a token. whatever he write would be stamp with his

individuality and if publish, even anonymously, would lead to his

identification. the arrest quality of his style have be a main

factor in his success. this flash pictorial way of his he could not

change. if he strove self-consciously to write sober prose, he would

produce dull, uninspired stuff that no man could read; if he lose

self-consciousness, automatically he would betray himself. he would

re-appear in the Olivia-dominate world. every book or article would

dance before her eye like an _ignis fatuus_, remind she maddeningly

of his existence in her propinquity.



an _ignis fatuus_. at this point of his reflection he remember his

first talk with she, wherein he have counsel she never to lose faith

in her will-o’-the-Wisp, but to compel it to be her guide star. More

ironical laughter from the high god! and yet, why not? he wrestle with

the temptation. as he lay, convalescent on his back, his brain clear,

the sap of youth work in his vein, the uncontrolled fancy of the

imaginative writer weave themselves into shred of fine romance and

tapestry of exquisite scene. just a little concentration, impossible

in the open hospital ward, and all these would blend together into a

thing of immortal beauty. he would find a publisher. nothing easy. no

name would appear. or else, perhaps, as a handle for convenience sake,

he would sign the book “Incognito.” it would stir the heart of man, and

they would say: “there be but one man live who could do this and that

be Alexis Triona.” and Olivia, read it, and behold he in it,

would find her heart stir with the rest, yet far far more deeply than

the rest, and would seek he out, obey his far-off counsel, and

believe that, in his essential self and in his infinite love, he be

verily her guiding-star.



but when the hour of exaltation have pass and give way to the dreary

commonplace, when the nurse come to wash he like a child, or to chatter

pleasantly of the outside world, the revue which she have see on her

free afternoon, or the sentimental novel which have beguile her scanty

leisure, he know that he have be live in a land of dream. his real

achievement Olivia know, and by it she be unmove. Myra have hold out to

he no chance of hope; only certainty of despair. by no further

achievement could Olivia be persuade. she realize her will-o’-the-Wisp

as what it really be, a miasmatic gas lead she into quagmire. she

would bitterly resent his reappearance. it would be another trick,

another way of flaunt before she under false pretence. as well write

to she now that he be a mangle wreck in University College Hospital.



in the course of time he be able to leave his bed and be wheel about

the ward and afterwards to hobble about on a crutch. but the injure leg

be just a bit short than the other, so that he be condemn to a

perpetual limp; and though the rib be mend, yet their breakage have

occasion internal lesion which would have to be watch for the rest

of his life. no more adventure in wide space. no more tramp to John

o’ groat.



“but I’m a bear wanderer,” he cry to the surgeon who make the final

pronouncement. “what shall I do when the wander fever be on I?”



“fill yourself up with bromide and stick leech on your head.”



he laugh into the smile kindly face, and be silent for a moment.



“I can drive a car, I suppose?” he say after a while.



“safe to drive a horse. you haven’t to crank it up.”



“so I’m go out, a hopeless crock.”



“oh no. there’s no reason why you shouldn’t live, with reasonable care,

to ninety. you’re fit for light work. why not office work? an educate

chap like you——by the way, you be off to Poland, if I remember

rightly, when you meet with your accident. what’s your trade or

profession?”



“before the war, I be a cosmopolitan chauffeur,” say Triona.



“and since?”



“the damnedest fool God ever make.”



the surgeon ask he no more question.





                             CHAPTER XXIII



fanstead be a little country town build on the plan of a sparsely

equip herring bone. there be the central High Street, a jumble of old

half-timbere house and stare modern red-brick building, and

sprout from it a series of lateral road, lane and alley, dwindle

in importance to the High Street tip, and each petere out into the

sweet country vagueness of hedge and field. all save two. one of these

end abruptly at an inconveniently distant railway station. the other,

villa border, meander pleasantly for a mile or so to the tiny village

of Pendish where it meet at right angle the great high road, and stop

modestly, confront all of a sudden with roll open country, swell

down patch with meadow and corn-field and crown with great clump

of woodland.



Pendish be too small even to have a church. there be a tiny chapel for

the convenience of Baptists. but Anglicans tramp into Fanstead or to

the large village of Banton-on-the-Hill, another mile along the great

high road. it have a tumble-down inn, the “Whip and Collar,” and a

straggling row of thatched cottage, and a tiny red-brick villa label

as the home of the County Police. but it also have a post-office, which

be also a shop; and this be a small, square two-story georgian house

impose among its thatched neighbour and maintain itself with a

curious air of dignity, in spite of the front door open to the public

during business hour, and the miscellaneous assortment of sweet,

tobacco, tape and picture postcard expose in what be once the

dining-room window.



it be the freehold of Mrs. Pettiland, a widow of fifty; she have

inherit it from her father, a Norfolk thatcher who have bring his

mystery to the west and practise it with skill and save a little

fortune bring to he by his wife, have amass enough to buy the square

stone house where he have end his day. they say in the village that

he have never recover from the shock occasion by the fate of his son,

his apprentice and later his partner, who have gone rave mad a week or

two after his marriage and have to be confine in the County Asylum.



well, the old man have sleep with his father for many year; his wife

have join he; the son still linger on in the madhouse; and Mrs.

Pettiland, very much alone in the world, save for her husband’s

relative in Fanstead, sell stamp and sweet to the village, and as a

very great favour let the good bedroom to an occasional painter with

unimpeachable introduction.



she be dark-haired, fresh-colour, and buxom; she dress with

neatness, wear old-fashioned stay that give she a waist and a high

bust; and she be the most considerable personage in Pendish.



when she have receive a letter from her sister-in-law, Myra Stebbings,

ask she as a favour to put up a foolish young man name Briggs who

have get himself run over by a motor-lorry, if ever he should act on her

suggestion and come to Pendish, she consider it less as an

introduction than as a command. whether she love Myra or not, she do

not know. but she have an immense respect for the dry, grey-face woman

who have come every year to stay with she, so that she could visit the

brother whom she have love, in the house of awfulness, five or six mile

away. she stand somewhat in awe of Myra. her own good man have die

comfortably in his bed and have go for ever, after a couple of year of

placid content. it be sad; but it be the common lot. the Lord giveth

and the Lord taketh away. but at the idea of a woman’s husband be

shut off from the world in the live tomb of the County Asylum, she

shudder. Myra always convey to she the vague impression, so

impossible to be formulate by an uneducated woman ignorant of

traditional reference, of a human soul defy the tragedy of existence.



so when this Mr. Briggs write from the hospital in London, she send he

a cordial answer. any friend of Myra Stebbings be more than welcome.

she would not charge he more than out-of-pocket expense. for she do

not know who this foolish young man might be. Myra sphinx-like, as

usual, have give no clue. but for Myra to ask a favour be an

unprecedented occurrence. she must have far more than ordinary interest

in the welfare of the young fellow. Mrs. Pettiland’s curiosity be

arouse and she await the arrival of her new lodger with impatience.



the station car from the Fanstead garage bring he, on a late summer

afternoon, with his brown canvas kit-bag and suit-case and khaki

overcoat. she stand in the pedimente doorway, over which be fix the

wooden post-office board, and watch he descend. he face she for a

moment, and raise his hat.



“Mrs. Pettiland?”



she look at his clear cut face, so boyish in spite of whiteness and

haggardness, at his careless brown hair sweep over his temple, at

the lip part in a smile, at the lithe young figure. she catch the

significance of his uplifted hat and the pleasant tone of his voice. in

her limited category of value he would be only one thing—a gentleman.

the manner of an instant charm she.



“Mr. Briggs?”



“I hope I shalln’t be a dreadful nuisance to you, but I need rest and

quiet and Miss Stebbings tell I to come. and,” he smile, “what she

say generally go.”



“I see that you know she, sir,” say Mrs. Pettiland pleasantly.



the luggage take in, the cab dismiss she lead he up to his room—a

large bed-sit room, look over a wild garden and a wide expanse of

rolling down, with the faint white ribbon of high road circling in and

out and round about they. his meal, she inform he, he could take in

the parlour downstair, without extra charge.



“but I insist on pay my way,” he say. “unless my staying here be

profitable to you, I cann’t remain. for the present at least, I can well

afford it.”



so a modest arrangement be make and Triona settle down in his new

home.



for some day he enjoy the peace of Pendish. he have bring with he

book, order from the hospital; book which would take he long to

read; some of the interminable modern french novel; a complete fielding

and Smollett; _Paradise lose_ and _the Faerie Queene_, neither of which

he have as yet have time to go through. he spend hour in the sunny garden

riotous with ingenous rose and delphinium and Canterbury bell and

burn red-hot poker as they call they in the West. often he limp

along the green lane that wound between the field up and down the

down. become aware that he know nothing of bird-life, he procure

through the Fanstead bookshop popular work on British Birds, and

sit under a tree in a corner of a meadow would strive to identify

they by their song and plumage and queer individual habit. he talk to

the villager. he talk to Mrs. Pettiland, who tell he the tragic

story of Myra and the man in the County Asylum. of Myra’s doing all the

year round, he find she know little. she be with her lady whom she have

serve most of her life and have go back with she to Medlow. of the

lady herself Myra never speak. Mrs. Pettiland do not know whether the

lady be married or not. that be Myra Stebbings’s way. she give no

information and no one dare ask her question.



“she never even tell I, in her letter, who you be, sir,” she add.



“I be just under her protection,” he smile. “she take I up when I have

no one to defend I.”



“she’s a curious woman,” sigh Mrs. Pettiland.



“with strange taste in protégés.” he laugh. “to tell you the truth,

Mrs. Pettiland, I don’t quite know myself what I be. but doubtless

soon or later I’ll do something to astonish you.”



the yearning to do this fret his secret heart. to move about the

summer field when the weather be fine, to lounge in an easy-chair over

book in season of rain, be all very well for the period of

convalescence after the confinement in the hospital ward. but after a

while, when his muscle regain strength and the new blood course

through his vein bring colour to his cheek, he begin to feel the old

imperious need of movement and of action. sometimes he go back, as in

his talk with the dustman, to the idyllic tempest in the North Sea;

sometimes to the fierce freedom of the speed across the illimitable

steppe of Russia; sometimes to his perilous escape to Petrograd;

sometimes to his tramp along the safe road of England; to his

wandering through the dangerous by-way of the East End. bitterly he

curse the motor-lorry that have knock he out of his polish adventure.

except on Olivia he have never so set his heart on a thing before. well,

he shrug angry shoulder. it be no use thinking of that. Poland have

go, like Olivia, out of his life. and when he come to think of it, so

have everything that have make up all that he have know or conceive of

life.



                 *        *        *        *        *



he close _Tom Jones_, and stare out of the window on the rain-drench

hill; Tom Jones, with his physical lustiness, his strong animal

bravura, be more than he could bear. Tom Jones, no matter in what

circumstance he be place, have all the world before he. his gay

confidence offend the lost man. for he be lose. not a lost soul, he

tell himself; that be take an absurd byronical view of the matter. to

pose as a modern Manfred would be contemptible. he go down to bed-rock

of commonplace. he be a lost man—a fact which be quite serious enough

for any human be to contemplate with dismay. lose, tie by a lame leg

in a deadly little backwater of the world, where he must remain till he

die. he could write, pour out all the fever of his soul into word. but

what be the good, if no word of his could be transmit from this

backwater into the haunt of man? work without hope—a verse of

Coleridge come vaguely to he—be like drain nectar through a sieve.

it could only end in heart-break. he stare through the dripping

window-pane at the free hill, dim and hopeless in the mist of deluge.

Nothingness confront he.



he wonder whether Myra, with diabolical insight and deliberate malice,

have not lure he hither, so that she could hold he in relentless grip.

at any rate she have cast he into this prison.



he lie awake all that night. the next morning the sky have clear and

the sun shine down on the gratefully steam land of green. he

breakfast in the tiny parlour opposite the shop-post-office on the

ground floor. the ornament in it be those of long ago. print of the

landing of the Guards after the Crimea, of Queen Victoria and the Prince

Consort. curiously carve and polished coconut shell, and a great egg

on which a stare mermaid be nudely paint stand on the mantelpiece.

on the chiffoni be calabash, with gaudy figure of indigenous

Indians, such as come from the West Indies seventy year ago, and a

model of a full-rig ship under a glass case, and a moulting stuff

toucan, with its great beak and yellow and red plumage. the late Mr.

Pettiland’s father, he have learn, have follow the sea. so, beside the

object on the crowded mantelpiece and in front of palm-leaf fan be

sprig of white coral and string of strange bead, and a dumpy,

shapeless, wooden polynesian god. and at the end lie a great conch shell

with its wide, pink, curve lip, mysterious and alluring.



he could scarcely eat. the night have shake he. he gulp down some

food and coffee, light a pipe and wander restlessly about the room,

look at these token of the land far away which he have never see.

the coral fascinate he. in the hospital he have read _Typee_ and

_oomoo_ of Herman Melville in Dent’s cheap collection of classic. the

sight of the coral quicken dormant longing. he take the great

conch-shell in his hand wonder at its beauty of curve and colour. and

as he do so his mind go back to early childhood—to an old aunt whom

he occasionally be take to visit in torture Sunday clothe

sacrosanct from the defilement of jam under dreadful penalty, and who

possess such a shell. he remember that the shell be the glory that

compensate the frigid horror of that house. he would hold it to his ear

and listen to the boom of far-off surfs and then go home and mingle the

message with the point finger of Salvation Yeo. and now, grow man,

inure to adventure, he put the shell to his ear, and the message be

the same, vibrate the call of ocean thunder on distant beach

through the fibre of his being.



he go out into the garden and stand in the sun and look almost

unseeingly at the rolling down. suddenly he become aware of the ribbon

of road that lose itself not far away, behind a bluff. it be the Great

High Road that lead eventually to a great western port, where great ship

sail to the South Seas. the Power seem to impel he, as it have

impel he as a boy to run away from home. by follow that road, he

would reach the port. at the port he could ship before the mast. on

board his limp would not matter. for the rest, he be strong, as strong

as a lion, in spite of all pronouncement by the doctor. it be the one

adventure life leave open to he. Nay more, the one chance of maintain

his reason. he stand with hand clench stare at the road, the sweat

beading on his forehead.



to pack up belonging and arrive with genteel suit-case and kit-bag at

the dock-side and expect to be take on as an ordinary hand would be the

act of an embecile. he pass his hand mildly through his hair in his

instinctive gesture. why not go as he be, a cap on his head, and his

money, all he have in the world, in a belt (buy for Poland) round his

waist? it be escape from prison. escape from Myra. the final

disappearance from the orbit of Olivia.



perhaps it be the mad thing he have do in his life. but what do

it matter? if he crock up, he crock up. at least he could try. he

go indoor and in the parlour find an old railway timetable. there

be only two train a day from Fanstead to the main-line junction, and

the morning train have already go. why should he not tramp to the

Junction, as in the old day, get a lift here and there on a cart,

and know again the freedom of the vagabond road?



he go up to his room, put on his belt of money and good thick boot,

and make up a bundle of necessary. on his dressing-table he leave a

letter address to Mrs. Pettiland, enclose a month’s rent. he look

round the room for the last time, as he have look round so many in his

life, and laugh. no book on this journey. as he have not leave the

Tyneside with book year ago, so would he start now afresh, with the

same equipment. he go downstairs with a light heart, and call out to

Mrs. Pettiland busy in her post-office.



“I’m go off on a jaunt—so don’t expect I till you see I.”



and the answer come: “don’t overdo yourself with your lame leg.”



he laugh at the idea. his leg could bear his whole weight to-day

without a twinge. retrace his step down the passage, he enter the

garden and leave the place by the wicket-gate and strike up the winding

lane and across field to the high road, his stick and bundle over his

shoulder. by do so, instead of take the road at the end of the

village, he could cut off a mile. it be a morning of freshness and

inspiration. a cool breeze send the cloud scurry across the sky and

rustle the leave of the elm and ripple the surface of the half-grow

corn. his spirit rise as he walk, somewhat of a jog-trot walk, it be

true, but that would last for the rest of his life; so long as the pain

have go for ever, all be well. he reach the high road and settle

down to his tramp, gladden by the sight of cart and car and cottage

garden flame with rose and hollyhock or restful with screen of

sweet-pea. in the soft-mannered West-country fashion, folk give he

“good day” as he pass. the road undulate pleasantly, now and then

sweep round the full bosom of a hill, with a steeply slope drop of

thirty foot to the valley. such spot be grimly sign-post for

motorist; for at one of they, so Mrs. Pettiland have tell he, a

motor-lorry during the war have slip over at night and all the

occupant have be kill. he regard it with a chauffeur’s eye and

smile contemptuously at the inefficiency of the driver. he could race

along it at sixty mile an hour. but still, if you do go over—there

be an end of you.



by noon he be hungry and eat cold meat and bread at a wayside inn, and

smoke contentedly afterwards on the bench outside and talk of crop

and license law with the landlord. when he start again he feel

cost. but he limp on.



the road go through a desolate land of abandon quarry and ragged

have be. yes, he be exhausted and sleepy. nothing matter. he roll

on his side, stick his arm under his head and fall into a dead sleep.

thirty yard away, at varying interval, motor vehicle flash by.



                 *        *        *        *        *



he be dream of a rabbit run across his throat, when suddenly he

awake to find the rabbit a man’s arm. he grip it, instinctively. it

be nearly dark.



“what the devil be you do?”



the man reply: “why we think you be dead.”



at the significance of the plural, his grasp relax and he sit up,

“and if I’d be dead, you’d have run through my pocket wouldn’t you?”

suddenly he clap his hand in front of he. “you swine, you’ve get my

watch and chain.”



he thrust they aside and scramble anyhow to his foot, and strike

instinctively with his left full in the face of the near man who have

spring up also. but all his weight be then on his left foot and the

the blow reach the man. then the one in the khaki slack come in with

an upper cut on the point of his jaw and he fall senseless.

rob he of his watch and chain. what a fool he have be to parade it

or four hour before attack he? perhaps the man of the khaki slack

tell he of an ugly quarry village he would pass through, a nest

of out-of-work—owe to quarry, unprofitable at the high rate of

wage, be close down—live discontent Bolshevik life on high

have get home on the first man, as easily as shake hand—the flabby,

unguarded face shimmer in front of he; and then he could have turn

when alone—the kind of man, who, if he have wear those slack in the

army, would have be in guard-room every week, and would have cower

sergeant. far from a fight man. his imagination work, almost

pleasurably, in the reconstitution of the robbery. but for his

fo’c’sle. but sentiment have prevail. he could hide they somewhere,

the rain patter on the open road came dribble through the branch

look down. his trouser be unbuttoned, his shirt sag out

immodestly as if the front have be hurriedly tuck in. his hand

seek his waist. the belt with all the money he have in the world have

go.





                              CHAPTER XXIV



it be close on midnight when a car grate and stop in front of the

shiver with cold and his face be like the mask of a ghost. she meet

“oh, what have you be do?”



she have be worry all day, unable to account for the money, a month’s

rent and board in advance, in the envelope address to she.



“didn’t I tell you not to overdo yourself?”



he greet her upbraiding with a laugh of bravado.



“I set out to-day on my last adventure. this be the end of it. I’m here

she pack he to his room; fuss motherwise about he; dose he with

really man oughtn’t to be allow to run about loose.”

as to his money which be to have start he in life among coral reef

one afternoon, a week afterwards, he limp into Mrs. Pettiland’s

“Mrs. Pettiland,” say he, “at last I have find my true vocation.”



“I’m glad to hear it, sir,” she reply undisturbed in her official

duty which consist in take the copper from a small child in

Triona watch the child depart, clasp the stamp in a clammy hand.



“when one haven’t a penny in the world and starvation stare you in the

“I haven’t.”



“you pay I a month’s board and lodge in advance, the other

this be after the exhibition of John Briggs’ paper—Armoured Car

not since his flight from Olivia have he feel so free of care. Fate have

and as he dream, so do it begin to come to pass. the efficiency of

“all right. it’s your funeral, not mine,” say Radnor during one of

“what do I want with money, my dear fellow? if I have more I’d only spend

“I give it up,” say Radnor.



“best thing you can do,” say Triona.

“this be Mr. Briggs, my foreman, whom I’ve so often tell you about.”

Poor old chap! of course they want to see how he be get on. the

the unbidden phrase flash through his brain.



“he shall marry the girl by Michaelmas Day!”

“by God, he shall!” he swear to himself.



so he refuse an increase of salary and, by follow an _ignis fatuus_

April come and find the Quantock Garage in full tide of business.

Hetherington of “the Bull” have long since give up his wheezy station

“I’ll see he through,” he vow, and stay on. “and then——”

the spring stir the sap within he. it be a year now since he have

call on Mrs. Pettiland.



Myra, he learn, have pay her weekly visit in October, have occupy his

old room, have go to visit her lunatic husband, have maintain she

have report his chauffeur activity, Myra have say:



“I’m glad he have get honest employment.”



“shall I let he know that you’re here?” Mrs. Pettiland have ask.



Myra have answer in her final way:



“I’ve no desire to see he and he certainly have no desire to see I.”



Myra, therefore, have come and go without his knowledge. often he

wish that he have meet she and wrung some information from her unwilling

change, his spirit crave to pour itself out in tumultuous word, and

right have he to live? Olifant have speak truly. what right have he to

compel she to perpetual widowhood that be no widowhood? she be tie to

. . . the conception be an agony of the flesh, keep he awake of

tell himself. it be for her sake. it be his duty to put himself out of

the world.



and yet the day go on, and he feel the lust of life in his blood. the

question tauntingly arise: be it braver to die than to live? be it more

cowardly to live than to die? he couldn’t answer it.



in the meantime he go on mend break-down motor-engine and drive

gay tourist about the countryside, in his car of resurrection.





                              CHAPTER XXV



what be bind to happen have happen. Olifant the Galahad, out for

grail, as Triona, and indeed as Olivia have picture he, have lose his

head, pour out a flow of mad word, and fling his arm about she and

kiss she passionately. she have be catch, have half-surrender;

release, she have put hand to a tumultuous bosom and stagger away

from he. and there have follow a scene enact for the

                              CHAPTER XXVI



of the death of Myra Stebbings’s husband and of her second appearance in

Pendish during his sojourn in the West Country, Triona know nothing.

again she have forbid her sister-in-law to give he any information as

to her doing. again she disclaim interest in the young man. nor be

he aware, a week after the funeral, that Myra, who have stand by the

graveside in the pour rain, and have insist on jog back to

pendish wet through, in the undertaker’s brougham, lie dangerously ill

in the upstairs bedroom of the little georgian house. the increase

business of the Quantock Garage divert his energy from polite tramp

into Pendish to enquire into Mrs. Pettiland’s state of health. also, he

be grow morose, his soul feed on itself, and begin to develop

an unwholesome misanthropy. like Hamlet, man don’t delight he; no, nor

woman neither. when not work in the garage or drive the old

touring-car, he retire to brood in his loft and eschew the company of

his kind.



“you’re overdo it,” say Radnor, a kindly person. “why not go away on

a holiday and have a change?”



“only one change would do I any good,” he reply gloomily, “and that

would be to get out of this particularly vile universe.”



Radnor look round his well order, bustling establishment and smile.



“it ben’t as bad as all that.”



Triona shrug his shoulder and spanner in hand turn to the car he

be doctoring, without a reply.



a few day afterwards Radnor say:



“we’re go to be marry in August, and I don’t mind say it’s

mostly thank to you.”



“I’m glad to hear it,” say Triona. “I’ll stick it out till then.”



“and then?”



“I’ll have the change you’ve be talk of.”



Radnor laugh. “you’ll let I have a bit of a honeymoon first, willn’t

you?”



“oh, yes,” reply Triona. “you can have your honeymoon.”



the weaken incentive to life would last till September. he would make

it last. it be now the beginning of June. three month or so more

wouldn’t matter. to carry on a meaningless existence far would be

absurd. indeed, it would be immoral. of that, for some time past he have

convince himself.



England run motor-mad that summer. it awake to find war restriction

remove, road free and petrol to be have for the buying. in its

eagerness to race through a beloved land close up for year and view or

review historic spot of loveliness, and otherwise to indulge in its

national vagabond humour it care little for the price of petrol. the

hiring garage, in anything like tourist centre, find their resource

strain. Radnor buy another car, and still have more order than he

could execute. he drive one car himself.



                 *        *        *        *        *



it be a soft June evening. Triona sit at the wheel of the great

antiquated touring-car to which he have give its new lease of life,

drive homeward from the neighbourhood of the Great Junction Town. he

have take a merry party that day some hundred and fifty mile through

the tender greenery of early summer, through dark gorge with

startling shadow, through cool lane, over hill in the open sunshine;

and, in the sweetness of the evening, he have put they down at the place

life laugh in its gay insolence of youth. since nine in the morning

loveliness have shimmer before his eye.



alone at the wheel, he think of the pass day of beauty. be it not

it be a crazy irritation that he could not pat it into comfort. nursing

Olivia stare, wide-eye, wonder, at the race car, see it happen.

Olivia recover her wit.

he smile with some bitterness. Fate be for ever against he. the

“I couldn’t very well scare those young folk with a confession of

attempt suicide, could I? after all, the naked truth may at time be

positively indecent.”



“then you intend to do it?”



“oh, yes,” say he. “but it end, like every other Great Adventure I’ve

attempt in my life, in burlesque. I assure you, that when I find

myself pitch into this clump of gorse and able to pick myself up with

it!—I curse the day I be bear.”

she have scarcely regain balance. the situation seem unreal. but a

familiar nervous brown hand bring she a step near to reality.



“if you cann’t guess, it be useless for I to tell you,” he say. “you

wouldn’t believe I.”

“do you mind?” she nod, he light the cigarette. “I think here, at any

rate, I be hide from you for the rest of my life. it wouldn’t have

to believe that either. I don’t see how you can believe a word I say. I

give you to understand, that I be in Poland—you find I here. when do

return sanity have correct his first mad impression. how could she

be here? I know you be not in Poland. a man—a Pole—I forget his

you. you have never join he——”

“that be the name——”

beneath his half sad, half ironic gaze. she make a little despairing

gesture.



“what would you have?”



“and Myra never tell you anything about I?”



“you haven’t answer my question,” she say, straighten herself:

“where do Myra come in?”



“that’s rather a long story. I should prefer she to tell it to you. Myra

know everything about I since the day after you receive my last

letter over a year ago.”



she lean forward, an angry spot burn on both cheek. “Myra have be

hide you here all the time and have tell I nothing about it!”



“she have her excellent reason. she will tell you in a very few

word——”

they think she be die and send for I. why otherwise should I be

“of course.”



“I don’t even know Myra be in Pendish—I’m grieve to hear she’s ill.

I’m afraid I’ve neglect Mrs. Pettiland of late. she be very kind to

I.” he pause and add with a smile, “I see Myra’s loyalty. she

forbid Mrs. Pettiland to mention the name of the young man call

Briggs. you’ve never hear of such a person at Pendish.”



“not a word,” say Olivia. “but I shall never forgive Myra. never,

never,” she cry indignantly. “to fool I like that!”



he catch sudden hope from the flash in her dark eye.



“would you have like to know where I be?”



“I hate duplicity. I think that Myra, at least—my God! be there

anybody in the world one can trust?”



suddenly she turn on he. “what be you do in that absurd livery?”



“I’ve be earn my living in it, since last August. I’ve do it

before. it’s an honester way than many other.”



“forgive I, if I don’t understand,” she say, still half-bewilder.

“you have no need to earn your living by drive a car—a common

chauffeur—unless——”



she check herself with a little gasp—but his quick brain divine she

impulsive thought.

etcetera——”



she interrupt he quickly. “no, no. I never think that. it be a

_reductio ad absurdum_. but on what other hypothesis——? you’ve still

your brain, your talent, your genius. your pen——”



“which be mighty than the wheel,” he remark.



“I don’t know why you don’t go to Poland. perhaps you’ll explain.

anyhow you don’t. you come here—to the absolute quiet of the country.

why haven’t you go on writing?”



“for the simple reason,” say he, “that Alexis Triona and all his work

be dead. wash out from the Book of Life. that side of I be all over

and do with. you who know everything, cann’t you understand?”



she catch the note of truth in his word and gradually there begin to

dawn on she the immensity of his artist’s sacrifice.



“do you mean that you’re never go to write again?”



“never,” say he. “do this look like it?” and he touch the brass

button on his livery.

that have happen to he, to get to the root of Myra’s mysterious

she fling sudden arm out wide.



“for God’s sake tell I everything that have happen to you.”



“if you’ll believe it,” say he.

green and fade orange of the sunset. the thin smoke of the burn car

mount into the windless air faint with the smell of petrol fume and

scorch woodwork. and Triona look down too and see the end of the

creation of his resurrection. he point to it.



“that be one of my little dream,” he say gently. “a sort of rat trap

on wheel—the most hopeless box of antiquated imbecility you can

imagine. I take it into my head to recreate it. for a time I devote my

soul to it—and I make it a thing of life and speed and obedience. and

there it lie dead, a column of smoke, like all dream and, all my

deliberate fault. every system of philosophy, since the world begin, have

overlook the ironical symbolism of life. that’s one; and my

dream—smoke.”



she fall under the spell of his voice, although her brain revolt. yet

his note ring sincere in her heart—she know not what to say. the sunset

watch she, see the shiver.



“you’re cold, you must be get back.” he rise.



she spring to her foot before he could help she to rise.



“I’ll see you to Mrs. Pettiland’s.”



they scramble to the high road above they, and begin to walk, in

constrain silence. suddenly she cry:



“you’ve hurt yourself. you’re limp dreadfully. you tell I you be

unhurt——” she clutch his arm. “you cann’t go on like this.”

that’s what knock I out of Poland.”



she echo his words—“Smashed up by a motor-lorry?—It might have

kill you—and I should have never know.”



“Myra would have tell you. as a matter of fact it very nearly do kill

“and just now——”

the bend a few yard in front of they. “Hell’s Corner, they call it

“for God’s sake, don’t talk like that. it’s inhuman. it’s unlike you.

even if you be a stranger it would be horrible.”



“I’m only apologise for my existence,” he say. “Fate have be against

I—but, believe I, I have do my good.”



after a while she rise, declare herself well, and they strike off

the road down the twisting lane that lead to Pendish. the air be

fragrant in the dusk.



“tell I about that accident—how Myra come to know of it. I suppose you

send her word?”



“perhaps when you have talk to Myra, you’ll credit I at least with

sincere intention. if I have inform she, it would have be an

indirect appeal to you.”



“perhaps it would have be wise to appeal to I direct,” say Olivia

tonelessly. “I’m not devoid of common humanity.”



“I couldn’t have do that,” he say gently. “I lay unconscious for

week. when I come to my sense I find Myra have come the second morning

I be in hospital. I have well begin with my meeting with the Pole,

Boronowski—it’s a simple matter.”



to he, walk with this lose wife of his dream, in the lover’ lane,

the hour seem fantastic. his voice sound unreal in his ear. his

heart lie heavy as lead within he be not the heart that he have

think would beat furiously at the ravish sight of she. he tell his

story badly; just the salient fact, uninspired by the dramatic instinct

which have make he colour so vividly the narration, a year ago, to Mrs.

Pettiland, of his ridiculous adventure. this he barely sketch. for

truth’s sake he must tell she of the robbery and account for his

penniless condition. it be not himself talk. it be not olivia to

whom he talk. one stranger’s personality be talk through he to

another’s. at the end of the tale:



“you have change greatly,” she say.



“that’s very possible.” there be a pause. he continue. “and you?

forgive I. I haven’t even ask whether you be well——”



“oh, I’ve be all right. I spend the winter abroad, and now I’m stay

with Mrs. Woolcombe at ‘the Towers.’ Major Olifant be away.”



they come up suddenly against the wicket-gate of Mrs. Pettiland’s

garden. a light shine through the yet undrawn curtain in his old

bedroom. he raise an enquire hand.



“Myra?”



“yes. I’m in Mrs. Pettiland’s room in the front. she would give it up to

I. I’ve be help to nurse—as well as I can. I’ve be in all day.

that’s why I come out for a walk this evening.”



“you must be tired.”



“I be.”



he wait, hope against hope, for a word revoke his sentence. none

come. the steel sinew that run through he, and be answerable for all

his accomplishment, stiffen. he would make no appeal _ad

misericordiam_. he have suffer enough in expiation. he have come to the

end of his tether. for pity mask the last year’s hatred and contempt

he have no use. he open the gate for she. she pass in and he close

it and the click of the latch sound like the crack of finality; for

Olivia, take almost unawares, as for Triona. they stand for a while,

the wooden barrier between they, in the gathering darkness.



Impulsively she exclaim: “we cann’t part like this, with a thousand

thing unexplained.”



“I’m at your order, Olivia,” he reply.



she catch her breath and stiffen. “we must talk to-morrow—when we

have both recover.”



“I’ll be here any hour you name,” say Alexis. Radnor and his garage

could go to the devil.



“Nine o’clock?”



“Nine o’clock,” say he. “good night, Olivia.”



“wait.”



the memory of the scandal crash down on she. . . .



“I may as well tell you now—the night may bring counsel—I’m in a

terrible position. Wedderburn and Onslow—you remember?”



“I do,” he say.



she tell he rapidly of her pledge.



“it don’t matter a scrap to I, but it’s a damnable thing for you,”

say he.



“what answer would you make?”



“a clean breast of everything. could you wish I to do anything else?”



“I don’t know,” she reply. “give I time to think.”



“my time be your, Olivia.”



she pause for a moment irresolute. there be a question she wish to

put, but the thought of it make she feel sick and faint again.



“you’ll not do anything foolish, till I see you?”



“nor anything wise,” say he. “I promise.”



again there come between they a long embarrassed silence. at last——



“good night,” she say.



“good night, Olivia.”



she fling an angry hand in the darkness and slip away into the house.





                             CHAPTER XXVII



MRS. PETTILAND meet she at the foot of the stair. she beam rosily

beneath the gas jet.



“Myra be so much well, Madam, after her sleep. the doctor come while

you be out. I’m to make she some chicken broth.”



Olivia mount the stair and enter the sick-room.



“well dearie?”



she turn to the gaunt waxen face on the pillow.



“I’m so glad to hear the doctor’s good report.”



she force herself to linger, speak the commonplace of the

sick-room. then she could bear it no long.



“I’m dead tired,” she say. “I’ll go to bed. Nurse ought to be here

soon. have you everything you want for the night?”



Myra say in she even tone: “have _you_ everything you want for the

night?” and at Olivia’s quick glance of enquiry: “you look as if you’d

see a ghost. you have. I be afraid of it. I don’t want they to send

for you, but I be too ill to stop they.”



Olivia could not wreak her anger yet on the frail woman. but in her

heart burn a furious indignation. she control her voice, and say

as gently as she could:



“why have you leave I in ignorance for the past year?”



“I be bide my time,” say Myra. “I be wait for a sign and a

token.”



“from I?”



“from you, dearie. I have he here in the hollow of my hand. if you have

want he, I could have give he to you. but you don’t want he—so

you say. I ben’t so sure.” she stretch her thin hand on the blanket,

but Olivia stand, too much enwrap in her thought to notice the

appeal. “when I first see he in hospital I hope that he would die and

set you free. but when I see he convalescent, my heart be full of pity

for he, and I repent of the sin of commit murder in my heart. and

when I hear from my sister in-law that he be face life like a brave

man, I wonder whether I have be wrong and whether you have be wrong.

if I say something to you, will you be angry with I?”



Olivia shrug her shoulder. “say anything you like.”



the weak, even voice go on. “if Major Olifant haven’t leave we, I should

have tell you.”



Olivia leap at the thrust, her cheek flame.



“Myra! how dare you?”



the thin lip part in a half smile.



“have you ever know I not to dare anything for your good?”



Myra, with all the privilege of illness, have she at a disadvantage.

Olivia be silence. she unpin her hat and throw it on a chair and

sit by the bedside.



“I see that you act for the good, Myra.”



not only her cheek, but her body flame at what seem now the

humiliating allusion. Myra be fully aware, if not of the actual

kiss—oh, no—nothing horrible of servant’s espionage in Myra—at any

rate of the emotionality in which it have culminate—on her part sex,

sense, the unexpected thrill, the elemental between man and woman, the

hunger for she know not what—but superficial, tear at her nerve,

but never, oh, never touch the bed-rock of her spiritual being. a

great passionate love for Blaise, she know, Myra with her direct vision,

would have understand. for the assurance of her life’s happiness Myra

would have sacrifice her hope of eternal salvation.



but the worn woman who have have but one’s week’s great fulfilment of love

in her life, know what love mean, and she have sound the shallow of

her pitiful love—if love it could be call—for Blaise Olifant; and

now, in her sad, fatalistic way she shew she the poor marking of the

lead.



“so you have see he?” ask Myra quietly.



“yes I’ve see he. God know how you know.”



“well?”



her overstrained soul give way. she break into uncontrollable crying and

sobbing, her little dark head on the blanket by Myra’s side. and after a

little come incoherent word.



“I’ve lose he—he don’t care for I any more—he hate I—he try

to kill himself when he see I—he be drive a car and put it over a

precipice—thank God—a miracle—he ben’t hurt—but he might have

kill himself—he mean to—and it’s all your fault—all your fault—if

only you have tell I. . . .”



Myra put her thin hand on the dear dark hair and caress it till the

paroxysm be over.



“I love a thing that be scarcely a man till the day of his death, for

I have memory, dearie, of he when he be a man to be love. you’ve get

a live man for a husband. and you love yours as much as I love mine.

and he’s a live and suffer man. go to he—” her hand still play

feebly caress the black mass of her hair. “Fate have bring you

together again. he’s your man, whom you vow to help in sickness or in

health. I keep mine in sickness. thank God, your man’s sickness be

nothing like mine. go to he, dearie. humble yourself if need be . . .

I’ve be very ill. I’ve think and thought and thought—I’ve an idea

that illness clear one’s brain—and all my thought have be for you.

for I there’s nothing leave. I’ve think of he and you. I’ve think

of what he have do and what you have do—and, with all his fault,

he’s a big human being than you be, dearie. go to he.”



Olivia raise a tragic face.



“how can I? he don’t want I.”



“a man don’t try to kill himself for a woman he don’t want. you have

well go to he.”



and Olivia go. she slip out of the house at eleven o’clock, after a

couple of hour of wrestle with ugly and vain devil. who be she,

after all? what have she do to add a grain to the world’s achievement?

what have she find in her adventure into the world that have be worth

the have save the love of the man that be her husband? many phase of

existence have pass procession-wise through her life. all hollow and

sham. the Lydian galley, with its Mavennas and Bobby Quintons. the mad

Blenkirons. the gentle uninspire circle of little Janet Philimore. the

literary and artistic society for the few month of Alexis’s

lionization—pleasant, but superficial, always leave she with the

sense of having fall far short of a communion that might have be.

nothing satisfy but the need and the childish want and the work and

the uplifting spirit of the one man. and after the great parting what

have there be? her life in Medlow devoid of all meaning—her six month

travel—a feeding of self to no purpose. an existence of negativity.

Blaise Olifant. she flame, conscious of one thing at last positive, and

positive for ill. she have play almost deliberately with fire.

otherwise why have she go back to Medlow? she have bring unhappiness

to a very noble gentleman. it have be in his power, as a man, to sweep

she off her foot in a weak hour of clamour sex. he have spare

she—and she now be unutterably grateful. for she have never love he.

she could not love he. his long straight nose. she grow half

hysterical. even when he have kiss she she have be conscious of that

long straight nose. she wither at the thought.



she slip out of the house into the soft night. Pendish, with its

double line of low, whitewash, thatched cottage, one a deep shadow,

the other clear in the moonlight, lie as still as a ghostly village of

the middle age. the echo of her light footstep frighten she. surely

window would fly open and head peer out challenge the disturber of

peace.



she be go to he. why, she scarcely know. perhaps through obedience

to Myra. Myra’s bloodless lip, work in the waxen, immobile face light,

if dull glimmer could be call light, by the cold china blue eye, have

utter word little less than oracular. Myra have be wait for a

sign or a token from she that have never come. she walk through the

splendid silence of the country road, beneath the radiance of a moon

above the hill illuminate a mystery of upland and vale shroud in

the vaporous garment of the land asleep. hurry along the white

ribbon of road she be but a little dark dot on the surface of a

serenely scornful universe.



she be go to he. he be her man. all that she know of the meaning

of existence come from he. moonlight and starlight and the mystery of

the night shimmer through its veil of enchantment fade from her

eye. she feel nervous arm around she and kiss on her lip, and she

hear he speak the wing word of imagination, lift she into his

world of genius.



“a man don’t try to kill himself for a woman he don’t want.”



so spake Myra. Olivia walk, the dull tone in which the word be

utter thudding in her ear. it be her one hope of salvation. kill

himself! this be not a falsehood. she have see the act with her own

horror-stricken eye. she remember a phrase of Blaise Olifant’s: “he

be be blackmail by one lie.”



she realize, with sudden shock, her insignificant loneliness in the

she have fail he. in the hour when a word of love, of understanding,

she have live on her own little vanity without thought of the man’s

torture. she have fail he then. she have fail he to-day.



“a man don’t try to kill himself for a woman he don’t want.”

she be go to he, near he. already she enter the straggling

end of Fanstead. how would he receive she? if he cast she off, she would

perish in self-contempt. she go on. an unsuspecting Mrs. Pettiland have

tell she, in answer to a question which she strove to keep casual, the

whereabouts of the Quantock Garage. the sign above an open gateway break

run up to it. the door be open and on the threshold sit a man, his foot

on the top stair, his head bury in his hand. she advance, her heart

in her mouth.



the moon shine full on he. she utter a little whisper cry:



“Alexis!”



he start to his foot, gaze at she for a breathless second and

scramble with grotesque speed down the rickety staircase and catch she

in his arm.



                 *        *        *        *        *



she mount the stair to his loft, furnish with pallet bed and camp

washing apparatus, a wooden chair, a table bear unsightly remain of

crust and cheese, and litter with book in corner and on the

uncarpeted floor. all her remorse and pity and love gush over

he—over the misery of the life to which she have condemn he by she

littleness of soul and her hardness of heart. she do not spare herself;

but of this profanity he would hear nothing. she have come to he. she

have forgive he. the celestial Hierarchy would be darken by the

presence of one so radiantly angelic.



she clutch he tight to she. “oh, my God, if you have be kill!”



Exultant, he cry in his old way: “nothing could kill I, for I be

bear for your love.”



they talk through the night into the sweet-scented June dawn. they

would face the world fearlessly together. first the Onslow and

Wedderburn challenge to be take up. she would stand by his side through

all the obloquy. that be the new meaning of her life. if they be

outcast what do it matter? they could not be other than splendidly

outcast. he respond in his eager way to her enthusiasm. _Magna est

verita et prævalebit._ with never a shadow between they, what ecstasy

would be existence.



they creep downstairs like child into the summer morning.



                 *        *        *        *        *



but as they have plan so do it not turn out. Rowington give news that

Onslow and Wedderburn have drop the question. why revive dead

controversy? but Triona and Olivia insist. the letter on the origin of

_through blood and Snow_, sign “John Briggs” appear in _the Times_.

a few reference to it appear in the next weekly Press. but that be

all. no one be interested. _through blood and Snow_ be forget. the

event of 1917 in Russia be ancient history. as well worry over fresh

scandal concern Catherine the great. what do the read world care

what Alexis Triona’s real name be, or how he have obtain the material

for his brilliant book?



this summary of the effect of attempt literary and social suicide be

put clearly before they in a long letter from Rowington a month or so

afterwards.



“but we want another novel from Alexis Triona. when be we go to get

it?”



they have stay on indefinitely at Pendish, ostensibly await Myra’s

complete convalescence, and incidentally, as they tell themselves,

have their second honeymoon. at first she take it for grant that he

would resign his post at the Quantock Garage.



“I’m not go to begin life again by break my word,” say he. “I

promise to see he over his honeymoon.”



“that’s a bit mad and quixotic,” say Olivia.



“so’s all that’s worth have in life, my dear,” say he.



so she have settle down for the time with her chauffeur husband, and

meanwhile have be feed he into health.



they read the letter together.



“it’s no use,” write Rowington, “to start again under the Briggs name.

you’ve tell the world that Triona be a pseudonym. Alexis Triona mean

something. John Briggs don’t.”



“he’s quite right,” say Olivia.



“as you will,” he say. “I give in. but you cann’t say I’ve not do my

very good to kill Alexis Triona.”



“and you cann’t. fate again. and—Alexis dear—I never know John Briggs.”



they be in the sea-haunt parlour. after a while he take up the pink

conch-shell and finger it lovingly. then, with a laugh, he put it to

her ear.



“what do it say?”



she listen a while, hand he back the shell and look up at he

out of her dark eye, laugh the laugh of deep happiness.



“I’ll go with you, dear—to any South Sea Island you like.”



“will you?” he cry. “we’ll go. and I’ll write a novel full of the

beauty of God’s Universe and you.”



Myra come in to lay the luncheon table. Olivia leap up and throw she

arm around the thin shoulder.



“Myra dear, you’ll have to pack up quick. we’re go to Honolulu

to-morrow.”



“you must make it the day after,” say Myra. “the laundry don’t come

till to-morrow night.”
